DevOps Mastery & Interview Guide

A complete, production-grade learning handbook — from Linux basics to Kubernetes, Cloud, and beyond. Covers everything you need to go from zero to job-ready.

Beginner Friendly Interview Ready Production Grade Real-World Projects

🗺️

DevOps Learning Roadmap

All Levels
Phase 1 — Foundation (Month 1–2)

Linux, Networking, Git

Master the OS layer: file system, permissions, processes, shell scripting. Learn TCP/IP, HTTP/DNS basics. Learn Git branching and workflows.

Phase 2 — Containers & CI/CD (Month 2–4)

Docker, CI/CD Pipelines

Containerize apps, write Dockerfiles, understand Docker networking. Build CI/CD pipelines with Jenkins or GitHub Actions.

Phase 3 — Cloud & Orchestration (Month 4–7)

Kubernetes, AWS Core

Deploy workloads on Kubernetes. Learn AWS: EC2, S3, VPC, IAM, RDS. Understand Auto Scaling and Load Balancing.

Phase 4 — IaC & Config Mgmt (Month 7–9)

Terraform, Ansible

Write Terraform modules, manage state files, remote backends. Automate configuration with Ansible playbooks and roles.

Phase 5 — Observability & Security (Month 9–12)

Monitoring, DevSecOps, System Design

Set up Prometheus + Grafana, ELK stack. Integrate security scanning in pipelines. Design high-availability, zero-downtime systems.

🐧

A. Linux — Foundation of DevOps

Beginner → Advanced

Linux is the backbone of every DevOps environment. Virtually all servers, containers, and cloud instances run Linux. Mastering it is non-negotiable.

Real-world analogy: Linux is like a city's road network — everything runs on it whether you see it or not. DevOps engineers work on it daily.

File System Hierarchy

DirectoryPurposeExample Contents
/Root of everythingAll directories start here
/etcSystem config filesnginx.conf, passwd, hosts
/varVariable data (logs, caches)/var/log/syslog
/homeUser home dirs/home/ubuntu
/usrUser programs/usr/bin, /usr/lib
/tmpTemporary filesCleared on reboot
/procVirtual filesystem (kernel info)/proc/cpuinfo, /proc/meminfo
/optOptional softwareManual installs

File Permissions (Deep Dive)

# Permission format: [type][owner][group][other]
# Example: -rwxr-xr-- 1 ubuntu devops 2048 Apr 21 app.sh

# Breakdown:
-        # file type (- = file, d = dir, l = symlink)
rwx      # owner: read(4), write(2), execute(1) = 7
r-x      # group: read(4) + execute(1) = 5
r--      # other: read(4) = 4

# Octal: 754
chmod 754 app.sh
chmod u+x app.sh          # add execute for owner
chmod go-w sensitive.conf # remove write from group+other
chown ubuntu:devops app.sh
chown -R www-data /var/www
Never set 777 on files in production. Use least-privilege permissions. 644 for files, 755 for directories is the standard.

Essential Linux Commands

File Operations

ls -lah          # detailed list
find / -name "*.log" -mtime -7
cp -r src/ dst/
mv file.txt /tmp/
rm -rf /tmp/old/

Shell Scripting — Beginner to Advanced

Beginner: Basic Script Structure

#!/bin/bash
# Script: deploy.sh
set -e          # exit on any error
set -u          # treat unset vars as error
set -o pipefail # catch pipe errors

APP_NAME="myapp"
VERSION=$1      # first argument

echo "Deploying $APP_NAME version $VERSION"

if [[ -z "$VERSION" ]]; then
  echo "ERROR: version required"
  exit 1
fi

echo "Done!"

Intermediate: Functions, Loops, Error Handling

#!/bin/bash
set -euo pipefail

log() { echo "[$(date '+%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S')] $*"; }
die() { log "ERROR: $*" >&2; exit 1; }

check_service() {
  local service=$1
  systemctl is-active --quiet "$service" \
    && log "$service is running" \
    || die "$service is not running"
}

deploy_app() {
  local env=$1
  local services=("nginx" "postgresql" "redis")

  log "Starting deployment to $env"

  for svc in "${services[@]}"; do
    check_service "$svc"
  done

  # Pull latest code
  cd /var/www/myapp
  git pull origin main

  # Restart app
  systemctl restart myapp
  log "Deployment complete!"
}

deploy_app "${1:-production}"

Advanced: System Health Check Script

#!/bin/bash
set -euo pipefail

THRESHOLD_CPU=80
THRESHOLD_MEM=85
THRESHOLD_DISK=90
ALERT_EMAIL="ops@company.com"

get_cpu_usage() {
  top -bn1 | grep "Cpu(s)" | awk '{print $2}' | cut -d'%' -f1 | cut -d',' -f1
}

get_mem_usage() {
  free | grep Mem | awk '{printf "%.0f", $3/$2 * 100}'
}

get_disk_usage() {
  df -h / | awk 'NR==2 {print $5}' | tr -d '%'
}

send_alert() {
  local metric=$1 value=$2 threshold=$3
  echo "ALERT: $metric is at ${value}% (threshold: ${threshold}%)" \
    | mail -s "Server Alert: $metric High" "$ALERT_EMAIL"
}

main() {
  local cpu mem disk
  cpu=$(get_cpu_usage)
  mem=$(get_mem_usage)
  disk=$(get_disk_usage)

  echo "CPU: ${cpu}% | MEM: ${mem}% | DISK: ${disk}%"

  [[ ${cpu%.*} -gt $THRESHOLD_CPU ]] && send_alert "CPU" "$cpu" "$THRESHOLD_CPU"
  [[ $mem -gt $THRESHOLD_MEM ]]     && send_alert "Memory" "$mem" "$THRESHOLD_MEM"
  [[ $disk -gt $THRESHOLD_DISK ]]   && send_alert "Disk" "$disk" "$THRESHOLD_DISK"
}

main

0 0 1 * *     /opt/scripts/monthly.sh     # 1st of month

# Log cron output:
* * * * * /script.sh >> /var/log/cron.log 2>&1

# Network

nethogs              # per-process network usage

# Disk I/O
iotop                # per-process disk I/O
dstat -cdngy         # combined stats

# Tracing
strace -p PID        # system calls of process
lsof -p PID          # open files by process
perf top             # CPU profiling

Interview Questions — Linux

What is the difference between a process and a thread in Linux?
A process is an independent program with its own memory space, PID, and resources. A thread is a lightweight unit of execution within a process, sharing the same memory space. Threads are faster to create/switch between but share data, requiring synchronization (mutexes). In Linux, both are managed via clone() syscall; ps aux shows processes, ps -eLf shows threads.
How do you troubleshoot a server that is running out of disk space?
1) Run df -h to identify the full filesystem. 2) Run du -sh /var/log/* | sort -rh | head -20 to find the largest directories. 3) Check for large log files: find /var/log -size +100M. 4) Check deleted-but-open files: lsof | grep deleted — processes may hold references to deleted files. 5) Truncate logs or rotate: logrotate -f /etc/logrotate.conf. 6) Consider adding disk or archiving old data.
Explain Linux load average. What does "5.5 3.2 1.1" mean?
Load average shows the average number of processes in runnable or uninterruptible sleep state over 1, 5, and 15 minutes. "5.5 3.2 1.1" means load is increasing (1-min is highest). On a 4-core system, a load of 4.0 means 100% utilization — 5.5 means you're overloaded. Rule: if load average consistently exceeds number of CPU cores, investigate with top, vmstat, and iostat.
What happens when you run a command in Linux? (Process lifecycle)
1) Shell forks a child process via fork(). 2) Child calls exec() to load the binary. 3) Kernel creates process, assigns PID, allocates memory. 4) Process runs, makes system calls for I/O etc. 5) Process exits via exit(). 6) Parent receives SIGCHLD, calls wait() to collect exit status. If parent doesn't wait, child becomes a zombie process.
Scenario — Senior Level

SSH into a server and find it's unresponsive. How do you diagnose?

Step 1: Check if SSH daemon is running: systemctl status sshd

Step 2: Check system load: uptime or w

Step 3: Look for zombie processes or runaway processes: ps aux | awk '$8=="Z"'

Step 4: Check memory pressure: free -m and cat /proc/meminfo | grep -i swap

Step 5: Check OOM killer: dmesg | grep -i "killed process"

Step 6: Check disk full: df -h and df -i (inodes!)

Step 7: Review recent syslog: tail -200 /var/log/syslog

Mini Task: Write a bash script that monitors disk usage every 5 minutes and sends an alert if any partition exceeds 80% capacity. Use cron to schedule it.

B. Networking for DevOps

Beginner → Advanced

OSI vs TCP/IP Model

OSI LayerNameTCP/IP LayerProtocols/Examples
7ApplicationApplicationHTTP, HTTPS, DNS, SSH, FTP
6PresentationSSL/TLS, encoding
5SessionSession management
4TransportTransportTCP (reliable), UDP (fast)
3NetworkInternetIP, ICMP, routing
2Data LinkNetwork AccessEthernet, MAC addresses, ARP
1PhysicalCables, signals, NICs
Memory trick for OSI layers: "All People Seem To Need Data Processing" (Application → Physical)

TCP vs UDP Deep Dive

TCP — Reliable

  • Connection-oriented (3-way handshake)
  • Guaranteed delivery & ordering
  • Flow control & congestion control
  • Used by: HTTP, SSH, FTP, SMTP
  • Higher overhead

UDP — Fast

  • Connectionless — fire and forget
  • No guarantee of delivery or order
  • Used by: DNS, video streaming, gaming
  • Lower overhead, faster
  • App handles reliability if needed

TCP 3-Way Handshake

Client Server | | |------- SYN (seq=x) ------->| Client wants to connect | | |<-- SYN-ACK (seq=y,ack=x+1)| Server acknowledges | | |------- ACK (ack=y+1) ----->| Connection established | | |======= DATA TRANSFER =======| | | |------- FIN ---------------->| Client done |<------- FIN-ACK ------------| |------- ACK ---------------->| 4-way close

DNS Resolution Flow

Browser Recursive Root TLD Auth (your PC) Resolver Server Server Nameserver | | | | | |-- query: google.com ->| | | | | |-- ask root? -->| | | | |<-- .com NS ---| | | | |-- ask .com? ------------->| | | |<-- google.com NS ---------| | | |-- ask google's NS? ------------------->| | |<-- 142.250.x.x IP --------------------| |<--- 142.250.x.x ------|

HTTP vs HTTPS

# Check TLS certificate
openssl s_client -connect google.com:443 -showcerts
openssl x509 -in cert.pem -text -noout

# HTTP request anatomy
curl -v https://api.example.com/users \
  -H "Authorization: Bearer TOKEN" \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json"

# Status codes (must know for interviews!)
200 OK, 201 Created, 204 No Content
301 Permanent Redirect, 302 Temporary
400 Bad Request, 401 Unauthorized, 403 Forbidden, 404 Not Found
429 Too Many Requests
500 Internal Server Error, 502 Bad Gateway, 503 Service Unavailable, 504 Gateway Timeout

Load Balancing

AlgorithmHow it worksBest for
Round RobinDistributes requests sequentiallyUniform servers
Least ConnectionsSends to server with fewest active connectionsLong-lived connections
IP HashRoutes based on client IP hash (sticky sessions)Stateful apps
Weighted RRProportional to server capacityMixed capacity servers
Health-check awareOnly routes to healthy backendsProduction always

Networking Troubleshooting Commands

# Connectivity
ping -c 4 8.8.8.8                    # basic reachability
traceroute -n 8.8.8.8                # path tracing
mtr --report 8.8.8.8                 # combined ping+traceroute

# DNS
dig google.com                        # full DNS lookup
dig @8.8.8.8 google.com              # use specific DNS server
dig google.com +trace                # follow delegation chain
nslookup google.com

# Port/service
nc -zv host 80                       # test port open
telnet host 22                       # test connection
ss -tulpn | grep :80                 # what's on port 80

# Packet capture
tcpdump -i eth0 port 80 -w cap.pcap  # capture to file
tcpdump -i eth0 'host 10.0.0.1'      # filter by host
wireshark cap.pcap                   # GUI analysis

# Firewall
iptables -L -n -v                    # list rules
ufw status verbose                   # Ubuntu firewall status
ufw allow 22/tcp

Interview Questions — Networking

What is the difference between a reverse proxy and a load balancer?
A reverse proxy sits in front of servers, receiving client requests and forwarding them to backend servers — it hides server identity and can do SSL termination, caching, compression (e.g., Nginx as reverse proxy). A load balancer specifically distributes traffic across multiple servers for availability and scalability (e.g., AWS ELB). In practice, tools like Nginx and HAProxy can do both simultaneously.
A user reports the website is slow. How do you debug it?
Systematic approach: 1) Check if issue is DNS: dig to verify resolution time. 2) Check TTFB (Time to First Byte) with curl -w "%{time_total}". 3) Check server CPU/memory: top, free. 4) Check database queries (slow query log). 5) Check network latency with traceroute. 6) Check application logs for errors. 7) Check CDN or cache hit rates. 8) Use Chrome DevTools network waterfall for frontend analysis.
Mini Task: Use curl -w "@curl-format.txt" -o /dev/null -s URL to measure DNS lookup, connection, and transfer times for any website.
🌿

C. Git — Version Control Mastery

Beginner → Advanced
Real-world analogy: Git is like a time machine + parallel universe manager for your code. Every commit is a save point, every branch is an alternate timeline.

How Git Stores Data Internally

Working Staging Area Local Repo Remote Directory (Index) (.git/) (GitHub) | | | | |-- git add ---->| | | | |-- git commit --->| | | | |-- git push ---->| |<------- git checkout ------------| | | | |<-- git fetch ---| | | | | Git Objects (.git/objects/): BLOB = file content (by SHA hash) TREE = directory structure COMMIT = snapshot + metadata + parent pointer TAG = named commit reference

Essential Git Commands

# Setup
git config --global user.name "Your Name"
git config --global user.email "you@example.com"
git config --global core.editor "vim"

# Daily workflow
git status
git diff                   # unstaged changes
git diff --staged          # staged changes
git add -p                 # interactive staging (patch mode)
git commit -m "feat: add login page"
git log --oneline --graph --all

# Branching
git branch feature/login
git checkout -b feature/login  # create + switch
git switch -c feature/login    # modern syntax
git branch -d feature/login    # delete merged branch
git branch -D feature/login    # force delete

# Remote
git remote -v
git fetch origin
git pull origin main --rebase  # preferred in teams
git push -u origin feature/login
git push origin --delete old-branch

Merge vs Rebase

git merge

  • Creates a merge commit
  • Preserves full history
  • Non-destructive
  • Can clutter history
  • Use for: feature → main merges
git checkout main
git merge feature/login

git rebase

  • Rewrites commit history
  • Cleaner linear history
  • Destructive if shared
  • Never rebase public branches
  • Use for: updating feature branch
git checkout feature/login
git rebase main
Golden rule: Never rebase commits that have been pushed to a shared/public branch. It rewrites history and breaks other people's work.

Conflict Resolution — Step by Step

1
Run git merge feature/login — git reports conflicts
2
Run git status to see conflicting files
3
Open conflicting file — look for conflict markers:
<<<<<<< HEAD (current branch)
const port = 3000;
=======
const port = 8080;
>>>>>>> feature/login (incoming branch)
4
Edit the file to keep the correct version, remove all markers
5
git add app.js — mark as resolved
6
git commit — complete the merge

Branching Strategies

StrategyBranchesBest forRelease cycle
Git Flowmain, develop, feature/*, release/*, hotfix/*Versioned products, software releasesScheduled
Trunk-Basedmain only + short-lived feature branchesCI/CD, SaaS, high-frequency deploysContinuous
GitHub Flowmain + feature branches + PRsWeb apps, small teamsContinuous
GitLab Flowmain + environment branches (staging/prod)Multi-environment deploymentsContinuous

Advanced Git Commands

# Undo operations
git revert HEAD            # safe undo (creates new commit)
git reset --soft HEAD~1    # undo commit, keep staged
git reset --mixed HEAD~1   # undo commit, keep unstaged
git reset --hard HEAD~1    # undo commit, DISCARD changes

# Stash
git stash                  # save dirty work
git stash pop              # restore latest stash
git stash list             # list all stashes
git stash apply stash@{2}  # apply specific stash

# Cherry-pick
git cherry-pick abc1234    # apply specific commit
git cherry-pick A..B       # apply range of commits

# Bisect (find bad commit)
git bisect start
git bisect bad             # current commit is broken
git bisect good v1.0       # v1.0 was working
# git tests each commit automatically
git bisect reset

# Tags
git tag -a v1.0.0 -m "Release 1.0.0"
git push origin v1.0.0

# Clean up
git gc                     # garbage collect
git remote prune origin    # remove stale remote refs

Interview Questions — Git

What is git cherry-pick and when would you use it?
Cherry-pick applies a specific commit from one branch to another without merging the entire branch. Use case: a critical bug fix was committed on a feature branch — you cherry-pick just that commit to main/production without merging unfinished features. Be careful: cherry-picking creates a new commit with a different SHA, which can cause issues if the original branch is later merged.
What is git reflog and when would you use it?
Reflog records every change to HEAD (even resets and rebases). It's your safety net when you think you've lost commits. Run git reflog to see the history of HEAD movements, find the commit SHA you need, then git checkout SHA or git reset --hard SHA to recover. Reflog entries expire after 90 days by default.
⚙️

D. CI/CD — The Heart of DevOps

Intermediate
Real-world analogy: CI/CD is like a factory assembly line. Code goes in one end, automated tests run, and production-ready software comes out the other — reliably, every time.

Pipeline Lifecycle

Developer CI Server Registry Production | | | | |-- git push ---------->| | | | |-- checkout code | | | |-- lint/code quality | | | |-- unit tests | | | |-- build artifact | | | |-- integration tests | | | |-- security scan | | | |-- build Docker image | | | |-- push image -------->| | | |-- deploy to staging | | | |-- smoke tests | | | |-- deploy to prod ---->| | |<-- notification -------| | |

Jenkins — Freestyle vs Pipeline

Jenkinsfile (Declarative Pipeline)

pipeline {
  agent any

  environment {
    APP_NAME    = 'myapp'
    DOCKER_REPO = 'myorg/myapp'
    SONAR_TOKEN = credentials('sonar-token')
  }

  stages {
    stage('Checkout') {
      steps {
        git branch: 'main', url: 'https://github.com/org/repo.git'
      }
    }

    stage('Build') {
      steps {
        sh 'mvn clean package -DskipTests'
      }
    }

    stage('Test') {
      parallel {
        stage('Unit Tests') {
          steps { sh 'mvn test' }
        }
        stage('Lint') {
          steps { sh 'npm run lint' }
        }
      }
    }

    stage('Code Quality') {
      steps {
        withSonarQubeEnv('SonarQube') {
          sh 'mvn sonar:sonar'
        }
      }
    }

    stage('Quality Gate') {
      steps {
        timeout(time: 5, unit: 'MINUTES') {
          waitForQualityGate abortPipeline: true
        }
      }
    }

    stage('Docker Build & Push') {
      steps {
        script {
          def image = docker.build("${DOCKER_REPO}:${BUILD_NUMBER}")
          docker.withRegistry('https://registry.hub.docker.com', 'dockerhub-creds') {
            image.push()
            image.push('latest')
          }
        }
      }
    }

    stage('Deploy to Staging') {
      steps {
        sh """
          kubectl set image deployment/myapp \
            myapp=${DOCKER_REPO}:${BUILD_NUMBER} \
            --namespace=staging
          kubectl rollout status deployment/myapp --namespace=staging
        """
      }
    }

    stage('Deploy to Production') {
      when {
        branch 'main'
      }
      input {
        message "Deploy to production?"
        ok "Deploy"
      }
      steps {
        sh """
          kubectl set image deployment/myapp \
            myapp=${DOCKER_REPO}:${BUILD_NUMBER} \
            --namespace=production
          kubectl rollout status deployment/myapp --namespace=production
        """
      }
    }
  }

  post {
    always {
      junit '**/target/surefire-reports/*.xml'
      archiveArtifacts artifacts: 'target/*.jar', fingerprint: true
    }
    success {
      slackSend color: 'good', message: "Build ${BUILD_NUMBER} deployed successfully"
    }
    failure {
      slackSend color: 'danger', message: "Build ${BUILD_NUMBER} FAILED"
      emailext body: '${BUILD_LOG}', subject: 'Build Failed', to: 'team@company.com'
    }
  }
}

GitHub Actions

# .github/workflows/ci-cd.yml
name: CI/CD Pipeline

on:
  push:
    branches: [main, develop]
  pull_request:
    branches: [main]

env:
  REGISTRY: ghcr.io
  IMAGE_NAME: ${{ github.repository }}

jobs:
  test:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    strategy:
      matrix:
        node-version: [18, 20]   # test on multiple versions
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Setup Node.js ${{ matrix.node-version }}
        uses: actions/setup-node@v4
        with:
          node-version: ${{ matrix.node-version }}
          cache: 'npm'

      - name: Install dependencies
        run: npm ci

      - name: Run linter
        run: npm run lint

      - name: Run tests with coverage
        run: npm test -- --coverage

      - name: Upload coverage
        uses: codecov/codecov-action@v4

  security-scan:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    needs: test
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - name: Run Trivy vulnerability scanner
        uses: aquasecurity/trivy-action@master
        with:
          scan-type: 'fs'
          exit-code: '1'
          severity: 'CRITICAL,HIGH'

  build-and-push:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    needs: [test, security-scan]
    if: github.ref == 'refs/heads/main'
    permissions:
      contents: read
      packages: write

    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Log in to Container Registry
        uses: docker/login-action@v3
        with:
          registry: ${{ env.REGISTRY }}
          username: ${{ github.actor }}
          password: ${{ secrets.GITHUB_TOKEN }}

      - name: Extract metadata
        id: meta
        uses: docker/metadata-action@v5
        with:
          images: ${{ env.REGISTRY }}/${{ env.IMAGE_NAME }}
          tags: |
            type=sha
            type=raw,value=latest

      - name: Build and push Docker image
        uses: docker/build-push-action@v5
        with:
          context: .
          push: true
          tags: ${{ steps.meta.outputs.tags }}
          cache-from: type=gha
          cache-to: type=gha,mode=max

  deploy:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    needs: build-and-push
    environment: production  # requires manual approval in GitHub

    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - name: Deploy to Kubernetes
        uses: azure/k8s-deploy@v4
        with:
          manifests: k8s/deployment.yaml
          images: ${{ env.REGISTRY }}/${{ env.IMAGE_NAME }}:${{ github.sha }}

GitLab CI

# .gitlab-ci.yml
stages:
  - test
  - build
  - security
  - deploy

variables:
  DOCKER_TLS_CERTDIR: "/certs"
  IMAGE_TAG: $CI_REGISTRY_IMAGE:$CI_COMMIT_SHA

test:unit:
  stage: test
  image: node:20-alpine
  cache:
    paths: [node_modules/]
  script:
    - npm ci
    - npm test
  coverage: '/Coverage:\s(\d+\.\d+)%/'
  artifacts:
    reports:
      coverage_report:
        coverage_format: cobertura
        path: coverage/cobertura-coverage.xml

build:docker:
  stage: build
  image: docker:24
  services:
    - docker:24-dind
  script:
    - docker login -u $CI_REGISTRY_USER -p $CI_REGISTRY_PASSWORD $CI_REGISTRY
    - docker build -t $IMAGE_TAG .
    - docker push $IMAGE_TAG

security:scan:
  stage: security
  image: aquasec/trivy:latest
  script:
    - trivy image --exit-code 1 --severity HIGH,CRITICAL $IMAGE_TAG

deploy:staging:
  stage: deploy
  environment:
    name: staging
    url: https://staging.myapp.com
  script:
    - kubectl set image deployment/myapp myapp=$IMAGE_TAG -n staging
  only:
    - develop

deploy:production:
  stage: deploy
  environment:
    name: production
    url: https://myapp.com
  script:
    - kubectl set image deployment/myapp myapp=$IMAGE_TAG -n production
  when: manual
  only:
    - main

Interview Questions — CI/CD

What is the difference between Continuous Integration, Continuous Delivery, and Continuous Deployment?
CI: Developers merge code frequently (multiple times/day). Each merge triggers automated build and tests. Goal: detect integration issues early.
CD (Delivery): Every successful build is automatically prepared for production release, but requires manual approval to actually deploy.
CD (Deployment): Every successful build that passes tests is automatically deployed to production without human intervention. Requires excellent test coverage and monitoring.
How do you handle secrets in CI/CD pipelines?
Never hardcode secrets. Use: 1) CI/CD platform's secret store (GitHub Secrets, GitLab CI Variables, Jenkins Credentials). 2) HashiCorp Vault integration — pipeline fetches secrets at runtime. 3) AWS Secrets Manager / Parameter Store for cloud deployments. 4) Environment-specific secrets injected at deploy time. Always audit secret access, rotate regularly, and scan code with tools like truffleHog or gitleaks to detect accidentally committed secrets.
Your pipeline takes 45 minutes to run. How do you optimize it?
1) Parallelize: run tests in matrix, separate stages concurrently. 2) Cache dependencies (node_modules, Maven .m2, pip cache). 3) Use incremental builds — only rebuild changed modules. 4) Split test suites — unit tests fast, integration tests separate. 5) Use Docker layer caching (cache-from in build). 6) Run security scans async/post-merge rather than blocking. 7) Use faster runners (more CPU, SSD). 8) Fail fast — run quickest checks first.
Scenario — Intermediate

A deployment pipeline is failing intermittently (flaky tests). What do you do?

1) Identify which test fails and how often — track failure rate over 10+ runs.

2) Check for: race conditions, test order dependencies, external service dependencies (mock them).

3) Check for: timing issues — add explicit waits, not sleep().

4) Quarantine the flaky test: move to a separate job, fix it without blocking CI.

5) Add retry logic as temporary mitigation: retry: 2 in GitLab CI or retry step in GitHub Actions.

6) Fix root cause — make tests deterministic.

🐳

E. Docker — Containers Deep Dive

Intermediate → Advanced
Real-world analogy: Docker containers are like standardized shipping containers. The same container runs identically on a developer laptop, CI server, or cloud server — regardless of what's underneath.

How Containers Work Internally

Container vs VM: VM: Container: ┌──────────────────────┐ ┌──────────────────────┐ │ App A │ App B │ │ App A │ App B │ │ Libraries │ Libraries│ │ Libraries │ Libraries│ │ Guest │ Guest │ ├───────────┴──────────┤ │ OS │ OS │ │ Docker Engine │ ├───────────┴──────────┤ ├──────────────────────┤ │ Hypervisor │ │ Host OS Kernel │ ├──────────────────────┤ ├──────────────────────┤ │ Hardware │ │ Hardware │ └──────────────────────┘ └──────────────────────┘ Linux Kernel features that enable containers: - Namespaces: PID, Network, Mount, UTS, IPC, User isolation - cgroups: CPU, Memory, I/O resource limits - Union FS: layered filesystem (overlay2)

Dockerfile — Deep Explanation

# Multi-stage production Dockerfile
# Stage 1: Build
FROM node:20-alpine AS builder

WORKDIR /app

# Copy package files first (layer caching)
COPY package*.json ./
RUN npm ci --only=production

# Copy source code
COPY . .

# Build the app
RUN npm run build

# =============================================
# Stage 2: Production image (minimal)
FROM node:20-alpine AS production

# Security: run as non-root user
RUN addgroup -S appgroup && adduser -S appuser -G appgroup

WORKDIR /app

# Copy only what we need from builder
COPY --from=builder --chown=appuser:appgroup /app/dist ./dist
COPY --from=builder --chown=appuser:appgroup /app/node_modules ./node_modules
COPY --from=builder --chown=appuser:appgroup /app/package.json .

# Switch to non-root
USER appuser

# Expose port (documentation only)
EXPOSE 3000

# Health check
HEALTHCHECK --interval=30s --timeout=10s --start-period=5s --retries=3 \
  CMD node healthcheck.js || exit 1

# Use exec form (preferred — proper signal handling)
CMD ["node", "dist/server.js"]

Image Optimization Techniques

TechniqueImpactHow
Multi-stage builds70-90% size reductionSeparate build and runtime stages
Alpine base imagesnode:alpine = 7MB vs node = 350MBUse FROM node:20-alpine
Layer cachingFaster buildsCopy package.json before source code
.dockerignoreSmaller contextExclude node_modules, .git, tests
Minimize layersSmaller imageChain RUN commands with &&
No build tools in prodSecurity + sizeDon't install gcc, make in final stage

Docker Networking

# Network types
docker network ls
docker network create mynet --driver bridge

# bridge (default): containers communicate via virtual switch
# host: container uses host network directly (no isolation)
# none: no networking
# overlay: multi-host (used in Docker Swarm/K8s)

# Connect containers
docker run -d --name db --network mynet postgres:15
docker run -d --name app --network mynet -p 3000:3000 myapp

# Inside mynet, containers resolve each other by name:
# app can reach db at "db:5432"

# Inspect
docker network inspect mynet

Docker Volumes

# Types:
# Volume (managed by Docker) — PREFERRED for prod
docker volume create pgdata
docker run -d -v pgdata:/var/lib/postgresql/data postgres:15

# Bind mount (host path) — for dev
docker run -d -v $(pwd)/app:/app node:20 npm run dev

# tmpfs (in memory)
docker run -d --tmpfs /tmp:rw,size=100m myapp

# Volume operations
docker volume ls
docker volume inspect pgdata
docker volume prune   # remove unused volumes

Docker Compose — Full Stack Example

# docker-compose.yml
version: '3.9'

services:
  app:
    build:
      context: .
      target: production
    ports:
      - "3000:3000"
    environment:
      - NODE_ENV=production
      - DATABASE_URL=postgresql://user:pass@db:5432/mydb
      - REDIS_URL=redis://cache:6379
    depends_on:
      db:
        condition: service_healthy
      cache:
        condition: service_started
    restart: unless-stopped
    networks:
      - backend

  db:
    image: postgres:15-alpine
    volumes:
      - pgdata:/var/lib/postgresql/data
    environment:
      POSTGRES_DB: mydb
      POSTGRES_USER: user
      POSTGRES_PASSWORD_FILE: /run/secrets/db_password
    secrets:
      - db_password
    healthcheck:
      test: ["CMD-SHELL", "pg_isready -U user -d mydb"]
      interval: 10s
      timeout: 5s
      retries: 5
    networks:
      - backend

  cache:
    image: redis:7-alpine
    command: redis-server --maxmemory 256mb --maxmemory-policy allkeys-lru
    volumes:
      - redis_data:/data
    networks:
      - backend

  nginx:
    image: nginx:alpine
    ports:
      - "80:80"
      - "443:443"
    volumes:
      - ./nginx.conf:/etc/nginx/nginx.conf:ro
      - ./certs:/etc/nginx/certs:ro
    depends_on:
      - app
    networks:
      - backend

volumes:
  pgdata:
  redis_data:

networks:
  backend:
    driver: bridge

secrets:
  db_password:
    file: ./secrets/db_password.txt

Interview Questions — Docker

What is the difference between CMD and ENTRYPOINT in a Dockerfile?
CMD sets the default command and can be overridden: docker run myimage /bin/bash replaces CMD.
ENTRYPOINT sets the fixed executable that always runs — additional docker run args are passed as arguments to it.
Best practice: Use ENTRYPOINT for the main executable, CMD for default arguments. Example: ENTRYPOINT ["nginx"] CMD ["-g", "daemon off;"]. Use exec form (JSON array) to ensure proper signal handling — shell form wraps in /bin/sh -c which doesn't forward SIGTERM.
A Docker container uses too much memory and gets OOM-killed. How do you fix it?
1) Add memory limits: docker run -m 512m --memory-swap 512m myapp (--memory-swap = memory: swap disabled). 2) In compose: mem_limit: 512m. 3) Profile the app with docker stats to see actual usage. 4) Find memory leaks: exec into container, use top or heapdump tools. 5) In K8s: set resources.limits.memory. OOM kill appears in dmesg: "Killed process".
How do you reduce Docker image size in practice?
1) Multi-stage builds — build in node:20, copy output to node:20-alpine. 2) Use specific Alpine tags, not latest. 3) Combine RUN commands: RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y pkg && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*. 4) .dockerignore to exclude unnecessary files. 5) Don't install dev tools in production stage. 6) Use distroless base images for maximum security.
Mini Task: Build a 3-container Docker Compose setup: nginx (proxy) → Node.js app → Redis (session store). Use networks to isolate Redis from nginx direct access.

F. Kubernetes — Container Orchestration

Advanced
Real-world analogy: If Docker is a shipping container, Kubernetes is the entire port management system — it decides which ship (node) carries which containers, restarts broken ones, and scales up when demand increases.

Architecture — Control Plane & Worker Nodes

KUBERNETES CLUSTER ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │ CONTROL PLANE (Master) │ │ ┌────────────┐ ┌────────────┐ ┌───────────────────┐ │ │ │ API Server │ │ Scheduler │ │Controller Manager │ │ │ │(kube- │ │(kube- │ │(ReplicaSet, │ │ │ │ apiserver) │ │ scheduler) │ │ Deployment ctrl) │ │ │ └────────────┘ └────────────┘ └───────────────────┘ │ │ ┌────────────┐ ┌────────────┐ │ │ │ etcd │ │cloud-ctrl │ ← distributed KV store│ │ │ (cluster │ │ manager │ │ │ │ state) │ │ │ │ │ └────────────┘ └────────────┘ │ └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │ │ │ ┌───────┴──────┐ ┌──────┴──────┐ ┌─────┴───────┐ │ WORKER NODE 1│ │WORKER NODE 2│ │WORKER NODE 3│ │ ┌──────────┐ │ │ ┌─────────┐ │ │ ┌─────────┐ │ │ │ kubelet │ │ │ │ kubelet │ │ │ │ kubelet │ │ │ │kube-proxy│ │ │ │kube-prox│ │ │ │kube-prox│ │ │ │container │ │ │ │container│ │ │ │container│ │ │ │ runtime │ │ │ │ runtime │ │ │ │ runtime │ │ │ └──────────┘ │ │ └─────────┘ │ │ └─────────┘ │ │ Pod Pod Pod │ │ Pod Pod │ │ Pod Pod │ └──────────────┘ └─────────────┘ └─────────────┘

Core Objects

Pod — Smallest Deployable Unit

# pod.yaml
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: myapp
  labels:
    app: myapp
    version: "1.0"
spec:
  containers:
  - name: myapp
    image: myorg/myapp:1.0
    ports:
    - containerPort: 3000
    resources:
      requests:
        memory: "128Mi"
        cpu: "100m"        # 0.1 CPU core
      limits:
        memory: "256Mi"
        cpu: "500m"        # 0.5 CPU core
    readinessProbe:
      httpGet:
        path: /health
        port: 3000
      initialDelaySeconds: 10
      periodSeconds: 5
    livenessProbe:
      httpGet:
        path: /health
        port: 3000
      initialDelaySeconds: 30
      periodSeconds: 10
    env:
    - name: NODE_ENV
      value: production
    - name: DB_PASSWORD
      valueFrom:
        secretKeyRef:
          name: db-secret
          key: password

Deployment — Manage Replicas & Updates

apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: myapp
  namespace: production
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: myapp
  strategy:
    type: RollingUpdate
    rollingUpdate:
      maxSurge: 1         # can have 1 extra pod during update
      maxUnavailable: 0   # always keep all replicas running
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: myapp
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: myapp
        image: myorg/myapp:1.2
        resources:
          requests:
            memory: "128Mi"
            cpu: "100m"
          limits:
            memory: "256Mi"
            cpu: "500m"
      affinity:
        podAntiAffinity:           # spread across nodes
          preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
          - weight: 100
            podAffinityTerm:
              labelSelector:
                matchLabels:
                  app: myapp
              topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname

# Service YAML
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: myapp-svc
spec:
  selector:
    app: myapp          # routes to pods with this label
  ports:
  - port: 80            # service port
    targetPort: 3000    # container port
  type: LoadBalancer    # or ClusterIP, NodePort

---
# Ingress (HTTP routing at L7)
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: Ingress
metadata:
  name: myapp-ingress
  annotations:
    nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/rewrite-target: /
    cert-manager.io/cluster-issuer: letsencrypt-prod
spec:
  tls:
  - hosts: [myapp.com]
    secretName: myapp-tls
  rules:
  - host: myapp.com
    http:
      paths:
      - path: /api
        pathType: Prefix
        backend:
          service:
            name: api-svc
            port:
              number: 80
      - path: /
        pathType: Prefix
        backend:
          service:
            name: frontend-svc
            port:
              number: 80

HPA — Horizontal Pod Autoscaler

apiVersion: autoscaling/v2
kind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler
metadata:
  name: myapp-hpa
spec:
  scaleTargetRef:
    apiVersion: apps/v1
    kind: Deployment
    name: myapp
  minReplicas: 3
  maxReplicas: 20
  metrics:
  - type: Resource
    resource:
      name: cpu
      target:
        type: Utilization
        averageUtilization: 70
  - type: Resource
    resource:
      name: memory
      target:
        type: Utilization
        averageUtilization: 80
  behavior:
    scaleDown:
      stabilizationWindowSeconds: 300  # wait 5min before scaling down

Kubernetes Troubleshooting — Real Cases

Scenario — CrashLoopBackOff

Pod is in CrashLoopBackOff. How do you debug?

kubectl get pods                           # see status
kubectl describe pod myapp-abc123          # check Events section
kubectl logs myapp-abc123                  # current logs
kubectl logs myapp-abc123 --previous       # PREVIOUS container logs (key!)

# Common causes:
# 1. Application error on startup — check logs
# 2. Missing env var / secret — check describe Events
# 3. Liveness probe failing too quickly — check probe config
# 4. OOMKilled — check "Last State" in describe
# 5. Image pull failure — check imagePullPolicy, image name
Scenario — Pending Pod

Pod stuck in Pending state. How do you debug?

kubectl describe pod myapp-abc123
# Look at Events:
# "0/3 nodes are available: insufficient memory" → nodes don't have enough
# "did not match pod's node affinity/selector" → nodeSelector mismatch
# "persistentvolumeclaim not found" → PVC issue

# Check node resources
kubectl top nodes
kubectl describe nodes | grep -A5 "Allocated resources"
Scenario — Service Not Reachable

Service exists but pods can't reach it. Debug steps:

# 1. Check service selector matches pod labels
kubectl get svc myapp-svc -o yaml
kubectl get pods --show-labels | grep myapp

# 2. Check endpoints (should have IPs)
kubectl get endpoints myapp-svc
# If empty: selector doesn't match any running pods

# 3. Test from inside cluster
kubectl run tmp --image=busybox -it --rm \
  --restart=Never -- wget -O- http://myapp-svc:80

# 4. Check kube-proxy logs on nodes
kubectl logs -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-proxy

Interview Questions — Kubernetes

What is the difference between a Deployment and a StatefulSet?
Deployment: For stateless apps. Pods are interchangeable — any pod can be replaced. Storage is ephemeral. No stable network identity. Scale up/down freely.
StatefulSet: For stateful apps (databases, Kafka). Each pod has stable hostname (pod-0, pod-1), stable storage (PVC per pod), ordered startup/shutdown. Used for: PostgreSQL, MongoDB, Kafka, ZooKeeper. Scale-up creates pod-N+1, scale-down removes pod-N first.
What are liveness vs readiness probes and why do both matter?
Liveness: "Is this container alive?" — If it fails, K8s kills and restarts the container. Use for: detecting deadlocks, hung processes.
Readiness: "Is this container ready to receive traffic?" — If it fails, K8s removes the pod from Service endpoints (stops sending traffic) but doesn't restart. Use for: startup time, temporary overload, dependency not ready.
Both matter: without readiness, traffic hits pods not yet ready; without liveness, hung pods stay running receiving requests they can't handle.
Explain Kubernetes resource requests vs limits.
Requests: Minimum guaranteed resources. Used by scheduler to decide which node can fit the pod. If you request 256Mi, the node must have 256Mi available.
Limits: Maximum allowed. CPU limit = throttling (slowed, not killed). Memory limit = OOMKill (process killed).
Best practice: Set both. Requests should be your normal usage, limits at 2-3x requests. If requests > limits, pod won't schedule. If no limits set, pod can consume all node resources.
Mini Task: Deploy a Node.js app on Kubernetes with: 3 replicas, HPA (scale 3-10 at 70% CPU), a LoadBalancer service, ConfigMap for config, and Secret for DB password. Write all YAML files.
☁️

G. Cloud — AWS Focus

Intermediate → Advanced
Real-world analogy: AWS is like renting a city's infrastructure — instead of building your own power plant, roads, and buildings, you rent exactly what you need and pay per use.

PRODUCTION AWS ARCHITECTURE ┌─────────────────────────────┐ │ Route 53 │ ← DNS └──────────────┬──────────────┘ │ ┌──────────────┴──────────────┐ │ CloudFront CDN │ ← Edge cache └──────────────┬──────────────┘ │ ┌──────────────────────────┴──────────────────────────┐ │ VPC │ │ ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │ │ │ Public Subnets (2 AZs) │ │ │ │ ┌──────────────┐ ┌──────────────────────┐ │ │ │ │ │ NAT Gateway │ │ Application LB (ELB) │ │ │ │ │ └──────────────┘ └──────────┬───────────┘ │ │ │ └─────────────────────────────────│───────────┘ │ │ ┌──────────────────────────────────│──────────────┐ │ │ │ Private Subnets (2 AZs) │ │ │ │ │ ┌────────────────────────────────┘ │ │ │ │ │ Auto Scaling Group │ │ │ │ │ ┌──────────┐ ┌──────────┐ │ │ │ │ │ │ EC2 #1 │ │ EC2 #2 │ │ │ │ │ │ │ (t3.med) │ │ (t3.med) │ │ │ │ │ │ └──────────┘ └──────────┘ │ │ │ │ └─────────────────────────────────────────── │ │ │ │ ┌─────────────────┐ ┌─────────────────────┐ │ │ │ │ │ RDS Aurora │ │ ElastiCache Redis │ │ │ │ │ │ (Multi-AZ) │ │ (cluster mode) │ │ │ │ │ └─────────────────┘ └─────────────────────┘ │ │ │ └─────────────────────────────────────────────── ┘ │ └──────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │ ┌───────────┴────────────┐ │ S3 (static assets, │ │ backups, logs) │ └────────────────────────┘

EC2 — Elastic Compute Cloud

# Launch EC2 via AWS CLI
aws ec2 run-instances \
  --image-id ami-0c55b159cbfafe1f0 \
  --instance-type t3.medium \
  --key-name my-keypair \
  --security-group-ids sg-12345678 \
  --subnet-id subnet-12345678 \
  --iam-instance-profile Name=EC2-SSM-Role \
  --user-data file://userdata.sh \
  --tag-specifications 'ResourceType=instance,Tags=[{Key=Name,Value=myapp},{Key=Env,Value=prod}]' \
  --count 1

# User data script (runs on first boot)
cat userdata.sh
#!/bin/bash
yum update -y
yum install -y docker amazon-cloudwatch-agent
systemctl start docker
systemctl enable docker
usermod -a -G docker ec2-user

# Instance types cheat sheet
# t3/t4g: Burstable (dev, small workloads)
# m6i/m7g: General purpose (apps, medium DBs)
# c6i/c7g: Compute optimized (CPU-intensive)
# r6i/r7g: Memory optimized (large DBs, analytics)
# i3/i4i: Storage optimized (NoSQL, data warehousing)

S3 — Simple Storage Service

# S3 commands
aws s3 mb s3://my-unique-bucket --region us-east-1
aws s3 cp file.txt s3://my-bucket/folder/
aws s3 sync ./dist/ s3://my-bucket/static/ --delete
aws s3 ls s3://my-bucket/ --recursive --human-readable

# Bucket policy (public read for static website)
aws s3api put-bucket-policy --bucket my-bucket --policy '{
  "Version": "2012-10-17",
  "Statement": [{
    "Effect": "Allow",
    "Principal": "*",
    "Action": "s3:GetObject",
    "Resource": "arn:aws:s3:::my-bucket/*"
  }]
}'

# Versioning
aws s3api put-bucket-versioning --bucket my-bucket \
  --versioning-configuration Status=Enabled

# Lifecycle rule (archive after 30d, delete after 365d)
aws s3api put-bucket-lifecycle-configuration --bucket my-bucket \
  --lifecycle-configuration file://lifecycle.json

# Pre-signed URL (temporary access)
aws s3 presign s3://my-bucket/file.txt --expires-in 3600

IAM — Identity and Access Management

# Principle of least privilege
# BAD: attaching AdministratorAccess to an EC2 role
# GOOD: only the permissions the service actually needs

# Example: EC2 instance that only reads from specific S3 bucket
{
  "Version": "2012-10-17",
  "Statement": [
    {
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "s3:GetObject",
        "s3:ListBucket"
      ],
      "Resource": [
        "arn:aws:s3:::my-app-bucket",
        "arn:aws:s3:::my-app-bucket/*"
      ]
    },
    {
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "ssm:GetParameter",
        "ssm:GetParameters"
      ],
      "Resource": "arn:aws:ssm:us-east-1:123456789:parameter/myapp/*"
    }
  ]
}

# Create role, attach policy, assign to EC2
aws iam create-role --role-name MyAppRole \
  --assume-role-policy-document file://trust-policy.json
aws iam put-role-policy --role-name MyAppRole \
  --policy-name MyAppPolicy --policy-document file://policy.json
aws iam create-instance-profile --instance-profile-name MyAppProfile
aws iam add-role-to-instance-profile \
  --instance-profile-name MyAppProfile --role-name MyAppRole

VPC — Virtual Private Cloud

ComponentPurposeKey Points
VPCIsolated networkCIDR block, e.g. 10.0.0.0/16
SubnetSub-network within VPCPublic or private, tied to AZ
Internet GatewayVPC → InternetFor public subnets
NAT GatewayPrivate → Internet (outbound only)Expensive, per-AZ, no inbound
Route TableRouting rules0.0.0.0/0 → IGW (public), → NAT (private)
Security GroupInstance-level firewall (stateful)Inbound + outbound rules
NACLSubnet-level firewall (stateless)Rules must be set for in AND out

Auto Scaling & Load Balancing

# Auto Scaling Group configuration
aws autoscaling create-auto-scaling-group \
  --auto-scaling-group-name myapp-asg \
  --launch-template LaunchTemplateName=myapp-lt,Version='$Latest' \
  --min-size 2 \
  --max-size 10 \
  --desired-capacity 3 \
  --target-group-arns arn:aws:elasticloadbalancing:... \
  --vpc-zone-identifier "subnet-aaa,subnet-bbb"  # multiple AZs

# Scaling policy (target tracking)
aws autoscaling put-scaling-policy \
  --policy-name cpu-target-policy \
  --auto-scaling-group-name myapp-asg \
  --policy-type TargetTrackingScaling \
  --target-tracking-configuration '{
    "PredefinedMetricSpecification": {
      "PredefinedMetricType": "ASGAverageCPUUtilization"
    },
    "TargetValue": 60.0
  }'

Cost Optimization Tips

AWS costs can spiral if unmanaged. These practices can reduce bills by 40-70%.
  • Right-sizing: Use AWS Compute Optimizer — most instances are over-provisioned by 50%+
  • Reserved Instances / Savings Plans: 1-3 year commitment = 30-70% discount vs On-Demand
  • Spot Instances: 70-90% cheaper for fault-tolerant workloads (batch, dev, CI/CD workers)
  • S3 storage classes: Move to Infrequent Access after 30 days, Glacier after 90 days
  • Delete unused resources: Unattached EBS volumes, idle Elastic IPs, old snapshots
  • CloudWatch billing alerts: Set alarms at $50, $100, $500 thresholds
  • Use AWS Cost Explorer: Monthly review — identify top cost drivers

AWS vs Azure vs GCP Quick Comparison

ServiceAWSAzureGCP
ComputeEC2Virtual MachinesCompute Engine
ServerlessLambdaAzure FunctionsCloud Functions
Container OrchestrationEKSAKSGKE (most mature)
Object StorageS3Blob StorageCloud Storage
Managed DatabaseRDSAzure SQLCloud SQL
IAMIAMAzure AD / RBACCloud IAM
DNSRoute 53Azure DNSCloud DNS
CDNCloudFrontAzure CDNCloud CDN
Market share (2024)~31%~25%~11%

Interview Questions — Cloud

What is the difference between Security Groups and NACLs in AWS?
Security Groups: Instance-level, stateful (if you allow inbound on 80, return traffic is automatic), only allow rules, no deny rules. Changes take effect immediately.
NACLs: Subnet-level, stateless (must explicitly allow return traffic), support both allow and deny rules, rules evaluated in number order. Good for blocking specific IPs subnet-wide.
Use both: NACLs for coarse-grained subnet protection, Security Groups for fine-grained instance control.
How does AWS Auto Scaling decide when to scale in/out?
Based on CloudWatch alarms tied to metrics (CPU, memory, custom metrics, SQS queue depth, etc.). Target tracking is simplest — set CPU target at 60%, ASG maintains it by adding/removing instances. Scale-out is aggressive (fast); scale-in has a cooldown period (default 300s) to prevent thrashing. Warm-up period ensures new instances aren't counted in metrics until they're ready. Always use multiple AZs for high availability.
🏗️

H. Terraform — Infrastructure as Code

Intermediate → Advanced
Real-world analogy: Terraform is like an architect's blueprint — you describe the desired state of your infrastructure, and Terraform figures out how to build/change it to match.

Terraform Workflow

Write HCL Code → terraform init → terraform plan → terraform apply → terraform destroy (main.tf) (download (show what (make it (tear it providers) will change) happen) down)

Complete AWS Project Example

# main.tf
terraform {
  required_version = ">= 1.6.0"
  required_providers {
    aws = {
      source  = "hashicorp/aws"
      version = "~> 5.0"
    }
  }
  backend "s3" {
    bucket         = "my-terraform-state"
    key            = "prod/terraform.tfstate"
    region         = "us-east-1"
    encrypt        = true
    dynamodb_table = "terraform-locks"  # state locking
  }
}

provider "aws" {
  region = var.aws_region
  default_tags {
    tags = {
      Environment = var.environment
      ManagedBy   = "Terraform"
      Project     = var.project
    }
  }
}

# VPC
module "vpc" {
  source  = "terraform-aws-modules/vpc/aws"
  version = "5.0.0"

  name = "${var.project}-${var.environment}-vpc"
  cidr = "10.0.0.0/16"

  azs             = ["us-east-1a", "us-east-1b", "us-east-1c"]
  private_subnets = ["10.0.1.0/24", "10.0.2.0/24", "10.0.3.0/24"]
  public_subnets  = ["10.0.101.0/24", "10.0.102.0/24", "10.0.103.0/24"]

  enable_nat_gateway = true
  single_nat_gateway = false  # one per AZ for HA
  enable_dns_hostnames = true
}

# EC2 Instance
resource "aws_instance" "app" {
  count         = var.app_instance_count
  ami           = data.aws_ami.amazon_linux.id
  instance_type = var.instance_type
  subnet_id     = module.vpc.private_subnets[count.index % length(module.vpc.private_subnets)]

  vpc_security_group_ids = [aws_security_group.app.id]
  iam_instance_profile   = aws_iam_instance_profile.app.name
  key_name               = var.key_pair_name

  root_block_device {
    volume_size = 30
    encrypted   = true
  }

  user_data = base64encode(templatefile("${path.module}/userdata.sh.tpl", {
    environment = var.environment
    app_version = var.app_version
  }))

  tags = {
    Name = "${var.project}-app-${count.index + 1}"
  }
}

# RDS
resource "aws_db_instance" "main" {
  identifier        = "${var.project}-${var.environment}-db"
  engine            = "postgres"
  engine_version    = "15.4"
  instance_class    = "db.t3.medium"
  allocated_storage = 100
  storage_encrypted = true

  db_name  = var.db_name
  username = var.db_username
  password = random_password.db.result

  multi_az               = true
  db_subnet_group_name   = aws_db_subnet_group.main.name
  vpc_security_group_ids = [aws_security_group.rds.id]

  backup_retention_period = 7
  deletion_protection     = true
  skip_final_snapshot     = false
  final_snapshot_identifier = "${var.project}-final-snapshot"
}

resource "random_password" "db" {
  length  = 32
  special = true
}

# Store DB password in Secrets Manager
resource "aws_secretsmanager_secret" "db_password" {
  name = "${var.project}/${var.environment}/db-password"
}
resource "aws_secretsmanager_secret_version" "db_password" {
  secret_id     = aws_secretsmanager_secret.db_password.id
  secret_string = random_password.db.result
}

# Data source
data "aws_ami" "amazon_linux" {
  most_recent = true
  owners      = ["amazon"]
  filter {
    name   = "name"
    values = ["amzn2-ami-hvm-*-x86_64-gp2"]
  }
}

Terraform State Management

# Remote backend setup (do this FIRST in new project)
# Create S3 + DynamoDB manually, then configure backend

aws s3api create-bucket --bucket my-terraform-state --region us-east-1
aws s3api put-bucket-versioning --bucket my-terraform-state \
  --versioning-configuration Status=Enabled
aws s3api put-bucket-encryption --bucket my-terraform-state \
  --server-side-encryption-configuration '...'
aws dynamodb create-table --table-name terraform-locks \
  --attribute-definitions AttributeName=LockID,AttributeType=S \
  --key-schema AttributeName=LockID,KeyType=HASH \
  --billing-mode PAY_PER_REQUEST

# State commands
terraform state list                    # list all resources
terraform state show aws_instance.app   # show resource details
terraform state mv aws_instance.app aws_instance.web  # rename
terraform state rm aws_instance.old     # remove from state (don't destroy)
terraform import aws_instance.app i-1234567890  # import existing resource

# Workspaces (manage multiple environments)
terraform workspace new staging
terraform workspace select production
terraform workspace list

Interview Questions — Terraform

What happens if two team members run terraform apply simultaneously?
State corruption can occur — both may read the same state and apply conflicting changes. Solution: use DynamoDB state locking with S3 backend. When one apply runs, it acquires a lock in DynamoDB. The second apply waits (or fails with lock error). If a lock gets stuck (apply crashed), manually remove it: terraform force-unlock LOCK_ID. Always use remote backend with locking in teams — never use local state files.
What is terraform taint and when do you use it?
terraform taint resource.name marks a resource for destruction and recreation on the next apply — even if its configuration hasn't changed. Use when: a resource is in a bad state (misconfigured EC2, corrupted DB), a resource needs to be replaced without changing config, you want to force a new key pair or certificate. In Terraform 1.0+, preferred approach is terraform apply -replace="resource.name" which does the same in one step.

I. Ansible — Configuration Management

Intermediate
Real-world analogy: Ansible is like a head chef giving instructions to multiple cooks simultaneously — you write the recipe once (playbook), and Ansible ensures every kitchen (server) follows it exactly.

Architecture

Control Node (your machine / CI server) │ │ SSH (agentless — no software needed on target) │ ┌────┴─────────────────────────────────────────┐ │ Inventory │ │ [web] [db] [all] │ │ web1.example.com db1 ... │ │ web2.example.com db2 │ └───────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │ │ web servers db servers (targeted separately)

Inventory

# inventory/hosts.ini
[webservers]
web1 ansible_host=10.0.1.10
web2 ansible_host=10.0.1.11

[dbservers]
db1 ansible_host=10.0.2.10 ansible_user=postgres

[production:children]
webservers
dbservers

[all:vars]
ansible_user=ubuntu
ansible_ssh_private_key_file=~/.ssh/prod-key.pem
ansible_python_interpreter=/usr/bin/python3

# Dynamic inventory (AWS)
# ansible-inventory -i aws_ec2.yaml --list

Playbook — Complete Deployment

# deploy-app.yml
---
- name: Deploy Application
  hosts: webservers
  become: yes
  vars:
    app_user: appuser
    app_dir: /opt/myapp
    app_port: 3000
    node_version: "20"

  pre_tasks:
    - name: Update apt cache
      apt:
        update_cache: yes
        cache_valid_time: 3600
      when: ansible_os_family == "Debian"

  tasks:
    - name: Install required packages
      package:
        name:
          - git
          - nginx
          - curl
        state: present

    - name: Install Node.js
      block:
        - name: Add NodeSource repo
          shell: curl -fsSL https://deb.nodesource.com/setup_{{ node_version }}.x | bash -
          args:
            creates: /etc/apt/sources.list.d/nodesource.list

        - name: Install Node.js
          apt:
            name: nodejs
            state: present

    - name: Create app user
      user:
        name: "{{ app_user }}"
        system: yes
        shell: /bin/false
        home: "{{ app_dir }}"

    - name: Create app directory
      file:
        path: "{{ app_dir }}"
        state: directory
        owner: "{{ app_user }}"
        group: "{{ app_user }}"
        mode: '0755'

    - name: Clone application
      git:
        repo: https://github.com/org/myapp.git
        dest: "{{ app_dir }}"
        version: "{{ app_version | default('main') }}"
      become_user: "{{ app_user }}"
      notify: restart myapp

    - name: Install npm dependencies
      npm:
        path: "{{ app_dir }}"
        production: yes
      become_user: "{{ app_user }}"

    - name: Deploy systemd service
      template:
        src: templates/myapp.service.j2
        dest: /etc/systemd/system/myapp.service
        mode: '0644'
      notify:
        - reload systemd
        - restart myapp

    - name: Deploy nginx config
      template:
        src: templates/nginx.conf.j2
        dest: /etc/nginx/sites-available/myapp
      notify: reload nginx

    - name: Enable nginx site
      file:
        src: /etc/nginx/sites-available/myapp
        dest: /etc/nginx/sites-enabled/myapp
        state: link

    - name: Start and enable services
      service:
        name: "{{ item }}"
        state: started
        enabled: yes
      loop:
        - nginx
        - myapp

  handlers:
    - name: reload systemd
      systemd:
        daemon_reload: yes

    - name: restart myapp
      service:
        name: myapp
        state: restarted

    - name: reload nginx
      service:
        name: nginx
        state: reloaded

  post_tasks:
    - name: Wait for app to be healthy
      uri:
        url: "http://localhost:{{ app_port }}/health"
        status_code: 200
      retries: 10
      delay: 5

Roles Structure

roles/
  nginx/
    tasks/
      main.yml       # main task list
    handlers/
      main.yml       # handlers
    templates/
      nginx.conf.j2  # Jinja2 template
    files/
      ssl.crt        # static files
    vars/
      main.yml       # role variables (high priority)
    defaults/
      main.yml       # default variables (lowest priority)
    meta/
      main.yml       # role dependencies

# Using roles in playbook
- hosts: webservers
  roles:
    - role: nginx
      vars:
        nginx_port: 80
    - role: nodejs
    - role: myapp

Key Ansible Commands

# Ad-hoc commands
ansible webservers -i hosts.ini -m ping
ansible all -m shell -a "df -h" -i hosts.ini
ansible webservers -m service -a "name=nginx state=restarted" --become

# Run playbook
ansible-playbook deploy-app.yml -i inventory/hosts.ini
ansible-playbook deploy-app.yml -i inventory/hosts.ini \
  --limit web1                    # target specific host
  --tags deploy                   # only run tagged tasks
  --skip-tags security           # skip specific tags
  --check                        # dry run
  --diff                         # show file diffs
  -e "app_version=1.2.3"        # extra vars
  -e @vars/prod.yml              # vars from file

# Vault for secrets
ansible-vault encrypt secrets.yml
ansible-vault decrypt secrets.yml
ansible-vault view secrets.yml
ansible-playbook site.yml --ask-vault-pass
ansible-playbook site.yml --vault-password-file ~/.vault_pass

J. Monitoring & Observability

Advanced
The three pillars of observability: Metrics (what), Logs (why), Traces (where). You need all three to fully understand production system health.

Prometheus + Grafana Architecture

Your App Prometheus Grafana AlertManager │ │ │ │ │ /metrics │ │ │ │ (expose metrics) │ │ │ │<─── scrape ───────│ │ │ │ │── PromQL query ─>│ │ │ │<─ metrics ───────│ │ │ │ │── dashboard ──> │ │ │── alert rule ───>│ │ │ │ │── fire alert ───>│ │ │ │ │── Slack/PagerDuty

Prometheus Configuration

# prometheus.yml
global:
  scrape_interval: 15s
  evaluation_interval: 15s

alerting:
  alertmanagers:
    - static_configs:
        - targets: ['alertmanager:9093']

rule_files:
  - "alerts/*.yml"

scrape_configs:
  - job_name: 'prometheus'
    static_configs:
      - targets: ['localhost:9090']

  - job_name: 'node-exporter'
    static_configs:
      - targets: ['server1:9100', 'server2:9100']

  - job_name: 'myapp'
    metrics_path: /metrics
    scrape_interval: 10s
    kubernetes_sd_configs:
      - role: pod
    relabel_configs:
      - source_labels: [__meta_kubernetes_pod_annotation_prometheus_io_scrape]
        action: keep
        regex: true

PromQL — Query Language

# Instant queries
up                                    # is target up (1/0)
http_requests_total                   # counter

# Rate (per-second rate over 5min window)
rate(http_requests_total[5m])

# Error rate
rate(http_requests_total{status=~"5.."}[5m]) /
rate(http_requests_total[5m])

# CPU usage per pod
100 - (avg by (instance) (irate(node_cpu_seconds_total{mode="idle"}[5m])) * 100)

# Memory usage
(node_memory_MemTotal_bytes - node_memory_MemAvailable_bytes) /
node_memory_MemTotal_bytes * 100

# P99 latency
histogram_quantile(0.99, rate(http_request_duration_seconds_bucket[5m]))

# Top 5 pods by CPU
topk(5, rate(container_cpu_usage_seconds_total[5m]))

Alert Rules

# alerts/app.yml
groups:
- name: app-alerts
  rules:
  - alert: HighErrorRate
    expr: |
      rate(http_requests_total{status=~"5.."}[5m]) /
      rate(http_requests_total[5m]) > 0.05
    for: 2m
    labels:
      severity: critical
    annotations:
      summary: "High error rate: {{ $labels.job }}"
      description: "Error rate is {{ $value | humanizePercentage }}"
      runbook_url: "https://wiki.company.com/runbooks/high-error-rate"

  - alert: HighMemoryUsage
    expr: |
      (node_memory_MemTotal_bytes - node_memory_MemAvailable_bytes) /
      node_memory_MemTotal_bytes * 100 > 85
    for: 5m
    labels:
      severity: warning
    annotations:
      summary: "Memory > 85% on {{ $labels.instance }}"

  - alert: PodCrashLooping
    expr: kube_pod_container_status_restarts_total > 5
    for: 5m
    labels:
      severity: critical
    annotations:
      summary: "Pod {{ $labels.namespace }}/{{ $labels.pod }} is crash-looping"

ELK Stack

# docker-compose for ELK
version: '3.9'
services:
  elasticsearch:
    image: docker.elastic.co/elasticsearch/elasticsearch:8.11.0
    environment:
      - discovery.type=single-node
      - xpack.security.enabled=false
      - "ES_JAVA_OPTS=-Xms1g -Xmx1g"
    volumes:
      - es_data:/usr/share/elasticsearch/data

  kibana:
    image: docker.elastic.co/kibana/kibana:8.11.0
    ports:
      - "5601:5601"
    environment:
      - ELASTICSEARCH_HOSTS=http://elasticsearch:9200

  logstash:
    image: docker.elastic.co/logstash/logstash:8.11.0
    volumes:
      - ./logstash/pipeline:/usr/share/logstash/pipeline

  filebeat:
    image: docker.elastic.co/beats/filebeat:8.11.0
    user: root
    volumes:
      - /var/log:/var/log:ro
      - /var/lib/docker/containers:/var/lib/docker/containers:ro
      - ./filebeat.yml:/usr/share/filebeat/filebeat.yml:ro

Interview Questions — Monitoring

What is the difference between metrics, logs, and traces?
Metrics: Numerical time-series data — CPU%, request count, latency p99. Low storage cost. Good for alerting on known failure modes.
Logs: Structured or unstructured text events — error messages, access logs. High storage cost. Good for debugging specific incidents.
Traces: End-to-end journey of a request through distributed services — shows where time is spent across microservices. Tools: Jaeger, Zipkin, AWS X-Ray. Together they provide full observability.
Production alert firing at 3AM: CPU at 95%. Walk me through your response.
1) Acknowledge alert (prevent escalation). 2) Open Grafana — check CPU trend (sudden spike or gradual?). 3) top / kubectl top pods — identify which process/pod is consuming CPU. 4) Check logs for errors or unusual patterns: kubectl logs -f pod-name. 5) Check if traffic increased: Grafana request rate dashboard. 6) If runaway process: restart pod/service (mitigate first, fix later). 7) If traffic spike: trigger manual scaling. 8) Post-incident: root cause analysis, adjust alert thresholds, add runbook.
🔒

K. DevSecOps — Security in DevOps

Advanced

Secure CI/CD Pipeline

# Security stages in pipeline
stages:
  - test
  - sast          # Static Application Security Testing
  - sca           # Software Composition Analysis (dependencies)
  - build
  - container-scan # Docker image vulnerability scan
  - dast          # Dynamic Application Security Testing
  - deploy

sast:
  image: semgrep/semgrep
  script:
    - semgrep --config=auto --json --output=semgrep.json .
  artifacts:
    reports:
      sast: semgrep.json

sca:
  image: owasp/dependency-check
  script:
    - dependency-check.sh --project myapp --scan . --format JSON
  allow_failure: false  # block on HIGH/CRITICAL CVEs

container-scan:
  image: aquasec/trivy
  script:
    - trivy image --exit-code 1 --severity HIGH,CRITICAL \
        --ignore-unfixed $IMAGE_TAG

Secrets Management

# HashiCorp Vault — store and retrieve secrets

# Authenticate from CI
export VAULT_ADDR='https://vault.company.com'
export VAULT_TOKEN=$(vault write -field=token auth/approle/login \
  role_id=$VAULT_ROLE_ID secret_id=$VAULT_SECRET_ID)

# Read secrets
DB_PASSWORD=$(vault kv get -field=password secret/prod/database)
API_KEY=$(vault kv get -field=key secret/prod/api)

# Kubernetes: External Secrets Operator
# ExternalSecret automatically syncs Vault → K8s Secret
apiVersion: external-secrets.io/v1beta1
kind: ExternalSecret
metadata:
  name: db-secret
spec:
  refreshInterval: 1h
  secretStoreRef:
    name: vault-backend
    kind: ClusterSecretStore
  target:
    name: db-secret
  data:
  - secretKey: password
    remoteRef:
      key: secret/prod/database
      property: password

IAM Best Practices

  • Least privilege: Only grant permissions actually needed — review quarterly
  • No root account usage: Create IAM users, enable MFA on root, then lock it away
  • Service accounts: Use IAM roles for EC2/Lambda, not access keys
  • Access key rotation: Rotate every 90 days, audit with IAM Access Analyzer
  • SCP (Service Control Policies): Organization-level guardrails via AWS Organizations
  • CloudTrail: Enable in all regions, immutable audit log of all API calls

Vulnerability Scanning

# Trivy (container + filesystem scanning)
trivy image nginx:latest
trivy image --severity HIGH,CRITICAL myapp:1.0
trivy fs .                       # scan filesystem
trivy k8s --report summary cluster  # scan K8s cluster

# Snyk
snyk test                        # check dependencies
snyk container test myapp:latest
snyk iac test terraform/         # IaC misconfigs

# OWASP ZAP (DAST)
docker run -t owasp/zap2docker-stable zap-baseline.py \
  -t https://staging.myapp.com -r report.html

# Detect secrets in code
gitleaks detect --source=. --verbose
truffleHog git file://. --since-commit HEAD~5
