GitHub Actions — Terraform CI/CD

# .github/workflows/terraform.yml
name: Terraform

on:
  push:
    branches: [main]
    paths: ['terraform/**']
  pull_request:
    branches: [main]
    paths: ['terraform/**']

permissions:
  id-token: write   # OIDC auth to AWS (no static keys)
  contents: read
  pull-requests: write

env:
  TF_VERSION: "1.7.0"
  AWS_REGION: "us-east-1"

jobs:
  terraform:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    defaults:
      run:
        working-directory: terraform/

    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Configure AWS via OIDC
        uses: aws-actions/configure-aws-credentials@v4
        with:
          role-to-assume: ${{ secrets.AWS_ROLE_ARN }}
          aws-region: ${{ env.AWS_REGION }}

      - name: Setup Terraform
        uses: hashicorp/setup-terraform@v3
        with:
          terraform_version: ${{ env.TF_VERSION }}

      - name: Terraform Format Check
        run: terraform fmt -check -recursive

      - name: Terraform Init
        run: terraform init
        env:
          TF_VAR_cloudflare_api_token: ${{ secrets.CF_API_TOKEN }}

      - name: Terraform Validate
        run: terraform validate

      - name: Run tfsec (security scan)
        uses: aquasecurity/tfsec-action@v1.0.3
        with:
          working_directory: terraform/

      - name: Run Checkov (IaC compliance)
        uses: bridgecrewio/checkov-action@v12
        with:
          directory: terraform/

      - name: Terraform Plan
        id: plan
        run: terraform plan -out=tfplan -input=false
        env:
          TF_VAR_cloudflare_api_token: ${{ secrets.CF_API_TOKEN }}

      - name: Post plan to PR
        if: github.event_name == 'pull_request'
        uses: actions/github-script@v7
        with:
          script: |
            const plan = '${{ steps.plan.outputs.stdout }}';
            github.rest.issues.createComment({
              issue_number: context.issue.number,
              owner: context.repo.owner,
              repo: context.repo.repo,
              body: '## Terraform Plan\n\`\`\`\n' + plan + '\n\`\`\`'
            })

      - name: Terraform Apply
        if: github.ref == 'refs/heads/main' && github.event_name == 'push'
        run: terraform apply -auto-approve tfplan
        env:
          TF_VAR_cloudflare_api_token: ${{ secrets.CF_API_TOKEN }}

Deploy & Verify

# 1. Initialize and plan
cd terraform-cloudflare-alb/
export TF_VAR_cloudflare_api_token="your_token"
terraform init
terraform plan -var-file=terraform.tfvars

# 2. Apply (creates everything in ~5 minutes)
terraform apply -var-file=terraform.tfvars

# 3. Outputs
terraform output
# alb_dns_name    = "myapp-prod-alb-123456.us-east-1.elb.amazonaws.com"
# cloudflare_ns   = ["ns1.cloudflare.com", ...]
# asg_name        = "myapp-prod-asg"
# rds_endpoint    = "myapp-prod-db.abc.us-east-1.rds.amazonaws.com"

# 4. Verify Cloudflare is proxying
curl -sI https://yourdomain.com | grep -i "server\|cf-ray\|cf-cache"
# Server: cloudflare
# cf-ray: 87abc...
# cf-cache-status: HIT  (after first request)

# 5. Test WAF
curl -A "sqlmap" https://yourdomain.com/api/users
# Expected: 403 Forbidden — WAF blocked bad user-agent

# 6. Verify origin is locked (ALB rejects non-Cloudflare)
curl -sI http://<ALB-DNS> --connect-timeout 5
# Expected: connection refused or 403

# 7. Check ASG health
aws autoscaling describe-auto-scaling-groups \
  --auto-scaling-group-names myapp-prod-asg \
  --query 'AutoScalingGroups[0].Instances[*].{ID:InstanceId,State:HealthStatus,AZ:AvailabilityZone}'
Expected outcomes:
• https://yourdomain.com loads, served from Cloudflare edge (cf-ray header present)
• Static assets cached at Cloudflare (cf-cache-status: HIT after second request)
• Direct ALB access returns 403 (origin locked to Cloudflare IPs only)
• ASG scales from 2 → N instances automatically under load
• tfsec + Checkov pass in CI with zero HIGH findings
• All infra reproducible: terraform destroy && terraform apply

Interview Questions — Cloudflare + Terraform + ALB

Why lock the ALB security group to Cloudflare IP ranges only?
Without this, anyone who discovers your ALB DNS name (via DNS history, certificate transparency logs, or scanning) can bypass Cloudflare entirely — hitting your origin directly and bypassing WAF, rate limiting, and DDoS protection. By restricting ALB ingress to Cloudflare's published IP ranges, the ALB refuses all non-Cloudflare traffic. Cloudflare also adds the CF-Connecting-IP header with the real client IP so your app can log it. Update the IP list periodically — Cloudflare publishes changes at cloudflare.com/ips. The Terraform variable makes this a one-line update + apply.
What is Cloudflare Full-Strict SSL mode and why is it important?
Flexible: Cloudflare ↔ Origin is HTTP (unencrypted) — dangerous.
Full: Cloudflare ↔ Origin is HTTPS but accepts self-signed certs — vulnerable to MITM between CF and ALB.
Full-Strict: Cloudflare ↔ Origin is HTTPS and validates the cert — requires a valid cert on the ALB (ACM cert). This is the only mode that gives true end-to-end encryption. In our setup: user ↔ CF = TLS (CF cert), CF ↔ ALB = TLS (ACM cert). Without Full-Strict, you have a false sense of security.
How do you manage Cloudflare API tokens securely in Terraform CI/CD?
Never hardcode in .tf files or tfvars committed to git. Use: 1) GitHub Actions Secrets → passed as TF_VAR_cloudflare_api_token environment variable. 2) HashiCorp Vault → CI fetches token at runtime. 3) AWS Secrets Manager → fetch in pipeline before terraform apply. 4) Mark the variable sensitive = true in Terraform so it never appears in plan output or state diffs. 5) Create a scoped API token in Cloudflare (Zone:DNS:Edit + Zone:Firewall:Edit only — not the global API key). Rotate every 90 days.

Project 7 — Production Kubernetes: Full-Stack App with GitOps, Monitoring & Security

Advanced / Senior

Deploy a production-grade microservices application on Kubernetes using industry best practices: Helm charts, ArgoCD GitOps, Prometheus + Grafana monitoring, cert-manager TLS, Network Policies, Pod Disruption Budgets, RBAC, and horizontal autoscaling. Everything is code — zero kubectl manual commands in prod.

Step 1 — Provision EKS with Terraform

# terraform/main.tf
module "eks" {
  source  = "terraform-aws-modules/eks/aws"
  version = "~> 20.0"

  cluster_name    = "myapp-prod"
  cluster_version = "1.30"

  vpc_id                         = module.vpc.vpc_id
  subnet_ids                     = module.vpc.private_subnets
  cluster_endpoint_public_access = true
  cluster_endpoint_public_access_cidrs = ["YOUR_OFFICE_IP/32"]

  # Cluster addons
  cluster_addons = {
    coredns                = { most_recent = true }
    kube-proxy             = { most_recent = true }
    vpc-cni                = { most_recent = true }
    aws-ebs-csi-driver     = { most_recent = true }
    aws-efs-csi-driver     = { most_recent = true }
  }

  # IRSA — IAM roles for service accounts (no static keys in pods)
  enable_irsa = true

  eks_managed_node_groups = {
    system = {
      name           = "system"
      instance_types = ["m6i.large"]
      min_size       = 2
      max_size       = 4
      desired_size   = 2
      labels         = { role = "system" }
      taints         = [{ key = "CriticalAddonsOnly", value = "true", effect = "NO_SCHEDULE" }]
    }
    app = {
      name           = "app"
      instance_types = ["m6i.xlarge", "m6a.xlarge"]  # multiple types for Spot
      capacity_type  = "SPOT"                          # 70% cheaper for app nodes
      min_size       = 3
      max_size       = 20
      desired_size   = 4
      labels         = { role = "app" }
    }
  }
}

# terraform/addons.tf — install cluster addons via Helm
resource "helm_release" "ingress_nginx" {
  name             = "ingress-nginx"
  repository       = "https://kubernetes.github.io/ingress-nginx"
  chart            = "ingress-nginx"
  version          = "4.10.0"
  namespace        = "ingress-nginx"
  create_namespace = true

  set { name = "controller.replicaCount";           value = "2" }
  set { name = "controller.metrics.enabled";        value = "true" }
  set { name = "controller.metrics.serviceMonitor.enabled"; value = "true" }
  set { name = "controller.service.annotations.service\\.beta\\.kubernetes\\.io/aws-load-balancer-type";
        value = "nlb" }
}

  name             = "cert-manager"
  repository       = "https://charts.jetstack.io"
  chart            = "cert-manager"
  version          = "v1.14.0"
  namespace        = "cert-manager"
  create_namespace = true
  set { name = "installCRDs"; value = "true" }
}

helm/myapp/templates/deployment.yaml

{{- range $name, $svc := .Values.services }}
---
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: {{ $name }}
  namespace: {{ $.Values.namespace }}
  labels:
    app: {{ $name }}
    version: {{ $.Values.image.tag | quote }}
spec:
  replicas: {{ $svc.replicaCount }}
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: {{ $name }}
  strategy:
    type: RollingUpdate
    rollingUpdate:
      maxSurge: 1
      maxUnavailable: 0
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: {{ $name }}
        version: {{ $.Values.image.tag | quote }}
      annotations:
        prometheus.io/scrape: "true"
        prometheus.io/port: {{ $svc.port | quote }}
        prometheus.io/path: "/metrics"
        checksum/config: {{ include (print $.Template.BasePath "/configmap.yaml") $ | sha256sum }}
    spec:
      serviceAccountName: {{ $name }}-sa
      securityContext:
        runAsNonRoot: true
        runAsUser: 1000
        fsGroup: 2000
        seccompProfile:
          type: RuntimeDefault
      containers:
      - name: {{ $name }}
        image: {{ $.Values.image.registry }}/{{ $.Values.image.repository }}/{{ $name }}:{{ $.Values.image.tag }}
        imagePullPolicy: Always
        ports:
        - containerPort: {{ $svc.port }}
        securityContext:
          allowPrivilegeEscalation: false
          readOnlyRootFilesystem: true
          capabilities:
            drop: ["ALL"]
        resources:
          requests:
            memory: {{ $svc.resources.requests.memory }}
            cpu:    {{ $svc.resources.requests.cpu }}
          limits:
            memory: {{ $svc.resources.limits.memory }}
            cpu:    {{ $svc.resources.limits.cpu }}
        readinessProbe:
          httpGet:
            path: /health/ready
            port: {{ $svc.port }}
          initialDelaySeconds: 10
          periodSeconds: 5
          failureThreshold: 3
        livenessProbe:
          httpGet:
            path: /health/live
            port: {{ $svc.port }}
          initialDelaySeconds: 30
          periodSeconds: 10
          failureThreshold: 3
        envFrom:
        - configMapRef:
            name: {{ $name }}-config
        - secretRef:
            name: {{ $name }}-secret
        volumeMounts:
        - name: tmp
          mountPath: /tmp
        {{- if $svc.extraVolumeMounts }}
        {{- toYaml $svc.extraVolumeMounts | nindent 8 }}
        {{- end }}
      volumes:
      - name: tmp
        emptyDir: {}
      affinity:
        podAntiAffinity:
          requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
          - labelSelector:
              matchLabels:
                app: {{ $name }}
            topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname
      topologySpreadConstraints:
      - maxSkew: 1
        topologyKey: topology.kubernetes.io/zone
        whenUnsatisfiable: DoNotSchedule
        labelSelector:
          matchLabels:
            app: {{ $name }}
{{- end }}

  - type: Resource

      name: memory

        value: 2
        periodSeconds: 60

helm/myapp/templates/networkpolicy.yaml

---
# API service — only accepts from ingress-nginx and frontend
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: api-netpol
  namespace: {{ .Values.namespace }}
spec:
  podSelector:
    matchLabels:
      app: api
  policyTypes: [Ingress, Egress]
  ingress:
  - from:
    - namespaceSelector:
        matchLabels:
          kubernetes.io/metadata.name: ingress-nginx
    - podSelector:
        matchLabels:
          app: frontend
    ports:
    - port: 3000
  egress:
  - to:
    - podSelector:
        matchLabels:
          app: postgres
    ports:
    - port: 5432
  - to:
    - podSelector:
        matchLabels:
          app: redis
    ports:
    - port: 6379
  - to:   # allow DNS
    - namespaceSelector: {}
    ports:
    - port: 53
      protocol: UDP
---
# Postgres — only from API
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: postgres-netpol
  namespace: {{ .Values.namespace }}
spec:
  podSelector:
    matchLabels:
      app: postgres
  policyTypes: [Ingress]
  ingress:
  - from:
    - podSelector:
        matchLabels:
          app: api
    ports:
    - port: 5432

Step 4 — ArgoCD GitOps Setup

# gitops/argocd/app-of-apps.yaml
# This single file tells ArgoCD to manage all other ArgoCD Applications
apiVersion: argoproj.io/v1alpha1
kind: Application
metadata:
  name: app-of-apps
  namespace: argocd
  finalizers:
    - resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io
spec:
  project: default
  source:
    repoURL: https://github.com/myorg/k8s-production-app
    targetRevision: main
    path: gitops/argocd
  destination:
    server: https://kubernetes.default.svc
    namespace: argocd
  syncPolicy:
    automated:
      prune: true
      selfHeal: true
    retry:
      limit: 5
      backoff:
        duration: 5s
        maxDuration: 3m
        factor: 2

---
# gitops/argocd/myapp.yaml
apiVersion: argoproj.io/v1alpha1
kind: Application
metadata:
  name: myapp-production
  namespace: argocd
spec:
  project: default
  source:
    repoURL: https://github.com/myorg/k8s-production-app
    targetRevision: main
    path: helm/myapp
    helm:
      valueFiles:
        - values.yaml
        - values-prod.yaml
  destination:
    server: https://kubernetes.default.svc
    namespace: production
  syncPolicy:
    automated:
      prune: true
      selfHeal: false   # prod: selfHeal off — human reviews diffs
    syncOptions:
      - CreateNamespace=true
      - ServerSideApply=true
  revisionHistoryLimit: 10

Step 5 — cert-manager: Free Let's Encrypt TLS

# ClusterIssuer — production Let's Encrypt
apiVersion: cert-manager.io/v1
kind: ClusterIssuer
metadata:
  name: letsencrypt-prod
spec:
  acme:
    server: https://acme-v02.api.letsencrypt.org/directory
    email: devops@myapp.com
    privateKeySecretRef:
      name: letsencrypt-prod-key
    solvers:
    - http01:
        ingress:
          class: nginx

---
# Ingress with auto-TLS
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: Ingress
metadata:
  name: myapp-ingress
  namespace: production
  annotations:
    cert-manager.io/cluster-issuer: letsencrypt-prod
    nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/ssl-redirect: "true"
    nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/proxy-body-size: "10m"
    nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/rate-limit: "100"
    nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/rate-limit-window: "1m"
spec:
  ingressClassName: nginx
  tls:
  - hosts: [myapp.com, www.myapp.com]
    secretName: myapp-tls-cert   # cert-manager creates this automatically
  rules:
  - host: myapp.com
    http:
      paths:
      - path: /api
        pathType: Prefix
        backend:
          service: { name: api, port: { number: 3000 } }
      - path: /
        pathType: Prefix
        backend:
          service: { name: frontend, port: { number: 80 } }

Step 6 — Monitoring: Prometheus ServiceMonitor + Grafana Alerts

# helm/myapp/templates/servicemonitor.yaml
apiVersion: monitoring.coreos.com/v1
kind: ServiceMonitor
metadata:
  name: myapp-metrics
  namespace: production
  labels:
    release: monitoring   # must match Prometheus selector
spec:
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: api
  endpoints:
  - port: http
    path: /metrics
    interval: 15s
    scrapeTimeout: 10s

---
# PrometheusRule — app-specific alerts
apiVersion: monitoring.coreos.com/v1
kind: PrometheusRule
metadata:
  name: myapp-alerts
  namespace: production
spec:
  groups:
  - name: myapp.rules
    rules:
    - alert: APIHighErrorRate
      expr: |
        rate(http_requests_total{namespace="production",app="api",status=~"5.."}[5m]) /
        rate(http_requests_total{namespace="production",app="api"}[5m]) > 0.01
      for: 2m
      labels:
        severity: critical
        team: backend
      annotations:
        summary: "API error rate above 1%"
        description: "Error rate is {{ $value | humanizePercentage }} — check API logs"
        runbook_url: "https://wiki.myapp.com/runbooks/api-errors"

    - alert: APIHighLatency
      expr: |
        histogram_quantile(0.99,
          rate(http_request_duration_seconds_bucket{app="api"}[5m])
        ) > 2
      for: 5m
      labels:
        severity: warning
      annotations:
        summary: "API p99 latency above 2s"

    - alert: PodRestarting
      expr: increase(kube_pod_container_status_restarts_total{namespace="production"}[1h]) > 3
      for: 5m
      labels:
        severity: warning
      annotations:
        summary: "Pod {{ $labels.pod }} restarted {{ $value }} times in 1h"

Step 7 — CI/CD: Build → Push → GitOps Promote

# .github/workflows/build.yml
name: Build and Promote

on:
  push:
    branches: [main]
    paths: ['apps/**']

jobs:
  build:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    strategy:
      matrix:
        service: [frontend, api]
    outputs:
      image-tag: ${{ steps.tag.outputs.tag }}

    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Generate image tag
        id: tag
        run: echo "tag=sha-${GITHUB_SHA::8}" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT

      - name: Login to GHCR
        uses: docker/login-action@v3
        with:
          registry: ghcr.io
          username: ${{ github.actor }}
          password: ${{ secrets.GITHUB_TOKEN }}

      - name: Build and push ${{ matrix.service }}
        uses: docker/build-push-action@v5
        with:
          context: apps/${{ matrix.service }}
          push: true
          tags: ghcr.io/myorg/${{ matrix.service }}:${{ steps.tag.outputs.tag }}
          cache-from: type=gha
          cache-to: type=gha,mode=max
          build-args: BUILD_DATE=${{ github.event.head_commit.timestamp }}

      - name: Scan image with Trivy
        uses: aquasecurity/trivy-action@master
        with:
          image-ref: ghcr.io/myorg/${{ matrix.service }}:${{ steps.tag.outputs.tag }}
          exit-code: '1'
          severity: 'CRITICAL'

  promote:
    needs: build
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
        with:
          token: ${{ secrets.GH_PAT }}

      # Update image tag in Helm values — ArgoCD auto-detects the git change and deploys
      - name: Update image tag in values-prod.yaml
        run: |
          sed -i "s/tag: .*/tag: \"${{ needs.build.outputs.image-tag }}\"/" \
            helm/myapp/values-prod.yaml
          git config user.name  "github-actions[bot]"
          git config user.email "github-actions[bot]@users.noreply.github.com"
          git add helm/myapp/values-prod.yaml
          git commit -m "chore: deploy ${{ needs.build.outputs.image-tag }} to prod"
          git push

Step 8 — RBAC: Least-Privilege Service Accounts

# Per-service ServiceAccount (no default SA with broad perms)
apiVersion: v1
kind: ServiceAccount
metadata:
  name: api-sa
  namespace: production
  annotations:
    # IRSA — maps SA to IAM role (for AWS SDK calls from pods)
    eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn: arn:aws:iam::123456789:role/myapp-api-role
automountServiceAccountToken: false   # only mount when actually needed
---
# Role — minimal permissions this SA needs
apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
kind: Role
metadata:
  name: api-role
  namespace: production
rules:
- apiGroups: [""]
  resources: ["configmaps"]
  resourceNames: ["api-config"]
  verbs: ["get"]
- apiGroups: [""]
  resources: ["secrets"]
  resourceNames: ["api-secret"]
  verbs: ["get"]
---
apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
kind: RoleBinding
metadata:
  name: api-rb
  namespace: production
subjects:
- kind: ServiceAccount
  name: api-sa
  namespace: production
roleRef:
  kind: Role
  name: api-role
  apiGroup: rbac.authorization.k8s.io

# Open http://localhost:3000 — import dashboard ID 15760 (K8s cluster)

# ArgoCD detects git change → runs helm upgrade → rolling update begins
kubectl rollout status deployment/api -n production -w
Final state of this K8s project:
• GitOps: every production change goes through git — zero kubectl in prod
• Auto-TLS: cert-manager issues and renews Let's Encrypt certs automatically
• Autoscaling: HPA scales frontend (3-10) and API (3-15) on CPU + memory
• Zero downtime: PDB ensures 67%+ pods always up during deploys/node drains
• Network isolation: NetworkPolicy denies all traffic except explicitly allowed paths
• Monitoring: Prometheus scrapes all pods, alerts fire to Slack via Alertmanager
• Security: non-root pods, read-only rootfs, IRSA (no static AWS keys in pods)
• Multi-AZ: topologySpreadConstraints spread pods across AZs and nodes

Interview Questions — Kubernetes Production

What is GitOps and how does ArgoCD implement it?
GitOps is a practice where Git is the single source of truth for both application code and infrastructure state. Any change to the cluster must be made via a git commit — never direct kubectl. ArgoCD implements this by continuously watching a git repository and comparing its state against the live cluster. If they diverge (someone ran kubectl manually, or a pod crashed), ArgoCD either alerts or auto-syncs back to the git state (selfHeal). Benefits: full audit trail (git log = deployment history), easy rollback (git revert), PR-based approval for production changes, and disaster recovery (re-apply the git repo to any cluster and it self-assembles).
Why use Pod Disruption Budgets and when do they activate?
PDBs prevent Kubernetes from removing too many pods simultaneously during voluntary disruptions — node drains (kubectl drain), cluster upgrades, or Cluster Autoscaler scale-down. Without PDB: draining a node could take down all 3 replicas if they happen to be on that node, causing outage. With minAvailable: 67% on a 3-replica deployment: at most 1 pod can be evicted at a time, always keeping 2 running. PDBs do NOT apply to involuntary disruptions (node hardware failure, OOMKill). They're essential for safe cluster upgrades and maintenance windows.
How does IRSA (IAM Roles for Service Accounts) work and why is it better than node-level IAM roles?
IRSA maps a Kubernetes ServiceAccount to an AWS IAM Role using OIDC federation. The pod's service account token is exchanged for temporary AWS credentials via AWS STS — no static access keys stored anywhere. Why better than node IAM roles: with node roles, ALL pods on that node inherit the role's permissions (a compromised pod gets all node permissions). With IRSA, each pod/service gets its own minimal IAM role — blast radius of a compromise is limited to exactly what that service needs. Setup: annotate the K8s ServiceAccount with eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn, configure the IAM role trust policy to allow the OIDC provider to assume it.
A production deployment is stuck — 0 of 3 pods running after a Helm upgrade. How do you debug and recover?
1) kubectl get pods -n production — check pod status (Pending? ImagePullBackOff? CrashLoop?). 2) kubectl describe pod <pod> — read the Events section for root cause. 3) kubectl logs <pod> --previous — previous container logs. 4) kubectl get events -n production --sort-by=.lastTimestamp — cluster events. 5) If it's a bad image: helm rollback myapp 1 -n production — rolls back to previous Helm revision instantly. 6) If it's a config issue: fix values, run helm upgrade again. 7) After recovery: check why CI let a bad image through — add better smoke tests in the promote workflow.
🛡️

M. SRE — Site Reliability Engineering

Senior Level
Real-world analogy: SRE is like aviation safety — planes fly millions of times and almost never crash not by luck, but because every failure is studied, automated checks exist, and humans are kept away from repetitive error-prone tasks.

SLI, SLO, SLA — The Foundation

TermDefinitionReal ExampleWho owns it
SLI (Indicator)The metric you measure% of HTTP requests returning 200 in <200msEngineering
SLO (Objective)Target value for your SLI99.9% of requests successful <200ms, measured monthlyEngineering + Product
SLA (Agreement)Legal contract with penalty if SLO breached"We guarantee 99.9% uptime or 10% credit"Business + Legal
Error Budget1 - SLO = allowed failure budget99.9% SLO = 0.1% budget = 43.8 min/month of downtimeEngineering

Error Budget — The Key SRE Concept

Monthly Error Budget (99.9% SLO): Total minutes/month: 43,800 Allowed failures: 43.8 minutes (0.1%) ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │ Budget Consumed │ Remaining │ │ ██████████████████░░░░░ │ 30 min used │ │ 30 min / 43.8 min │ 13.8 min left │ └─────────────────────────────────────────────┘ Budget HEALTHY → ship features fast, take risks Budget WARNING → slow down, prioritize reliability Budget EXHAUSTED → feature freeze, only reliability work Budget NEGATIVE → incident review, no new deploys until root cause fixed

Four Golden Signals (Google SRE Book)

Latency

Time to serve a request. Track both successful AND failed request latency. Use p50, p95, p99 — NOT averages (averages hide tail latency).

histogram_quantile(0.99,
  rate(http_duration_seconds_bucket[5m])
)

Traffic

How much demand your system handles. Requests/sec, queries/sec, messages/sec. Baseline it so you know what "normal" looks like.

rate(http_requests_total[5m])

Errors

Rate of failed requests — 5xx, timeouts, wrong content. Both explicit (500) and implicit (200 but wrong response body).

rate(http_requests_total
  {status=~"5.."}[5m]) /
rate(http_requests_total[5m])

Saturation

How "full" your service is — CPU %, memory %, queue depth, connection pool usage. Predict problems BEFORE they cause errors.

100 - avg(irate(
  node_cpu_seconds_total
  {mode="idle"}[5m])) * 100

Toil — What SREs Eliminate

Toil = manual, repetitive, automatable work that scales linearly with service growth. Google's rule: keep toil below 50% of SRE time — the rest is engineering work that permanently reduces future toil.
Toil ExampleSRE Automation Solution
Manually restarting hung servicesKubernetes liveness probes + auto-restart
Adding capacity when traffic spikesAuto Scaling Groups / HPA
Running the same deploy script 10x/dayCI/CD pipeline with one-click deploy
Manually rotating SSL certificatescert-manager auto-renewal
Checking server health every morningPrometheus alerts + Grafana dashboards
Cleaning up old log files weeklylogrotate + S3 lifecycle policies
Manually creating AWS resourcesTerraform + GitOps

Incident Management Process

1
Detect — Alert fires via PagerDuty/OpsGenie → on-call engineer paged
2
Respond — Acknowledge within SLA. Join incident Slack channel. Assign Incident Commander (IC)
3
Mitigate first — Restore service ASAP (rollback, restart, reroute). Don't investigate root cause while service is down
4
Communicate — Update status page, notify affected customers, internal Slack every 30 min
5
Investigate — After service restored, find root cause using metrics, logs, traces
6
Post-mortem — Blameless write-up within 48h: timeline, root cause, contributing factors, action items with owners and deadlines

Chaos Engineering

# Chaos Engineering: deliberately inject failures to test resilience

# AWS Fault Injection Simulator (FIS) — terminate random EC2
aws fis create-experiment-template --cli-input-json '{
  "description": "Terminate 1 random app EC2",
  "targets": {
    "appInstances": {
      "resourceType": "aws:ec2:instance",
      "resourceTags": {"Role": "app"},
      "selectionMode": "COUNT(1)"
    }
  },
  "actions": {
    "terminateInstances": {
      "actionId": "aws:ec2:terminate-instances",
      "targets": {"Instances": "appInstances"}
    }
  },
  "stopConditions": [{
    "source": "aws:cloudwatch:alarm",
    "value": "arn:aws:cloudwatch:...:alarm:ErrorRateHigh"
  }],
  "roleArn": "arn:aws:iam::123456789:role/FISRole"
}'

# Chaos Monkey (Netflix OSS) — randomly kill pods
# For Kubernetes: Chaos Mesh / Litmus Chaos

# Litmus ChaosEngine — kill random pods in namespace
apiVersion: litmuschaos.io/v1alpha1
kind: ChaosEngine
metadata:
  name: pod-kill-chaos
  namespace: production
spec:
  appinfo:
    appns: production
    applabel: "app=api"
    appkind: deployment
  chaosServiceAccount: litmus-admin
  experiments:
  - name: pod-delete
    spec:
      components:
        env:
        - name: TOTAL_CHAOS_DURATION
          value: "30"
        - name: CHAOS_INTERVAL
          value: "10"
        - name: FORCE
          value: "false"

On-Call Best Practices

  • Runbooks for every alert — never have an alert without a documented response procedure
  • Alert on symptoms, not causes — alert on "user-facing error rate" not "CPU high" (which may be harmless)
  • Actionable alerts only — if you can't do anything about an alert, delete it
  • On-call rotation — max 2 pages/night, compensate engineers for out-of-hours pages
  • Postmortem without blame — systems fail, not people; focus on process improvements
  • Track MTTR/MTTD — Mean Time To Detect, Mean Time To Resolve — improve both over time

Interview Questions — SRE

What is an error budget and how does it influence engineering decisions?
Error budget = 1 minus your SLO, expressed as allowed downtime/failure rate per period. A 99.9% SLO gives 43.8 minutes/month of allowed downtime. When budget is healthy, teams ship features aggressively. When budget is nearly exhausted, the team shifts focus to reliability work. When exhausted, feature releases pause until reliability improves. It converts the reliability vs velocity tension into an objective, data-driven conversation instead of a political one. Example: team wants to deploy a risky migration — check remaining budget first. If only 5 minutes left and migration carries 15-minute risk, delay it.
How do you write a good post-mortem?
Key sections: 1) Summary: 2-3 sentences — what happened, impact, duration. 2) Timeline: Minute-by-minute from first symptom to full resolution. 3) Root cause: The "5 Whys" — keep asking why until you reach a systemic cause, not a person. 4) Contributing factors: Things that made the incident worse (poor monitoring, insufficient testing, unclear runbooks). 5) Action items: Specific, assigned, with deadlines — not vague "improve monitoring" but "add p99 latency alert to api service by 2024-01-15 — Owner: Jane". Blameless: focus on systems and processes, never name individuals as the cause.
What is the difference between SRE and DevOps?
DevOps is a culture and set of practices (CI/CD, automation, collaboration). SRE is Google's specific implementation of DevOps principles with an engineering-first approach. Key SRE additions: quantitative reliability targets (SLOs), error budgets to balance velocity vs reliability, the 50% toil cap rule, and embedding software engineers in operations. DevOps is the philosophy; SRE is one opinionated implementation. Many companies adopt SRE practices within their DevOps culture.
⚡

N. AWS Lambda & Serverless Architecture

Intermediate → Advanced
Real-world analogy: Lambda is like a taxi — you only pay when you're actually riding (code executing), not when the car is parked waiting. No servers to manage, infinitely scalable, billed per 100ms of execution.

How Lambda Works Internally

Invocation Flow: Event Source Lambda Service Your Code (API GW / S3 / | (execution environment) SQS / EventBridge) | | | | | |-- event ------>| | | |-- cold start (if new) --->| | | (init: download code, | | | start runtime, run | | | init code outside | | | handler) | | |-- invoke handler() ------->| | | |-- execute | |<-- response --------------| |<-- response ---| Warm start: execution environment reused → handler called directly (~ms) Cold start: new environment spun up → adds 100ms-3s latency Concurrency: each simultaneous request gets its own execution environment

Lambda Function — Node.js Example

// handler.js — REST API backed by Lambda
const { DynamoDBClient, PutItemCommand, GetItemCommand } = require('@aws-sdk/client-dynamodb');
const { marshall, unmarshall } = require('@aws-sdk/util-dynamodb');

const client = new DynamoDBClient({ region: process.env.AWS_REGION });
const TABLE = process.env.TABLE_NAME;

// Initialized OUTSIDE handler — reused across warm invocations
const validateUser = (body) => {
  if (!body.name || !body.email) throw new Error('name and email required');
};

exports.handler = async (event) => {
  const method  = event.httpMethod;
  const path    = event.path;
  const headers = {
    'Content-Type': 'application/json',
    'Access-Control-Allow-Origin': '*'
  };

  try {
    if (method === 'POST' && path === '/users') {
      const body = JSON.parse(event.body);
      validateUser(body);

      const item = {
        userId: { S: crypto.randomUUID() },
        name:   { S: body.name },
        email:  { S: body.email },
        createdAt: { S: new Date().toISOString() }
      };

      await client.send(new PutItemCommand({ TableName: TABLE, Item: item }));
      return { statusCode: 201, headers, body: JSON.stringify(unmarshall(item)) };
    }

    if (method === 'GET' && event.pathParameters?.userId) {
      const result = await client.send(new GetItemCommand({
        TableName: TABLE,
        Key: { userId: { S: event.pathParameters.userId } }
      }));

      if (!result.Item) return { statusCode: 404, headers, body: JSON.stringify({ error: 'Not found' }) };
      return { statusCode: 200, headers, body: JSON.stringify(unmarshall(result.Item)) };
    }

    return { statusCode: 404, headers, body: JSON.stringify({ error: 'Route not found' }) };

  } catch (err) {
    console.error('Error:', err);
    const status = err.message.includes('required') ? 400 : 500;
    return { statusCode: status, headers, body: JSON.stringify({ error: err.message }) };
  }
};

# Terraform — full serverless API setup
resource "aws_lambda_function" "api" {
  function_name = "${var.project}-api"
  role          = aws_iam_role.lambda.arn
  handler       = "handler.handler"
  runtime       = "nodejs20.x"
  timeout       = 30
  memory_size   = 256

  filename         = data.archive_file.lambda_zip.output_path
  source_code_hash = data.archive_file.lambda_zip.output_base64sha256

  environment {
    variables = {
      TABLE_NAME = aws_dynamodb_table.users.name
      NODE_ENV   = var.environment

  # VPC config (if Lambda needs to access RDS in private subnet)

  #   security_group_ids = [aws_security_group.lambda.id]
  # }

  dead_letter_config {
    target_arn = aws_sqs_queue.lambda_dlq.arn
  }
}

data "archive_file" "lambda_zip" {
  type        = "zip"
  source_dir  = "${path.module}/../src"
  output_path = "${path.module}/lambda.zip"
}

# DynamoDB — on-demand (no capacity planning)
resource "aws_dynamodb_table" "users" {
  name         = "${var.project}-users"
  billing_mode = "PAY_PER_REQUEST"
  hash_key     = "userId"

  attribute { name = "userId"; type = "S" }

  point_in_time_recovery { enabled = true }
  server_side_encryption  { enabled = true }

  ttl { attribute_name = "expiresAt"; enabled = true }
}

# Lambda IAM role — least privilege
resource "aws_iam_role" "lambda" {
  name = "${var.project}-lambda-role"
  assume_role_policy = jsonencode({
    Version = "2012-10-17"
    Statement = [{
      Action    = "sts:AssumeRole"
      Effect    = "Allow"
      Principal = { Service = "lambda.amazonaws.com" }
    }]
  })
}

resource "aws_iam_role_policy" "lambda" {
  role   = aws_iam_role.lambda.id
  policy = jsonencode({
    Version = "2012-10-17"
    Statement = [
      {
        Effect   = "Allow"
        Action   = ["dynamodb:GetItem", "dynamodb:PutItem", "dynamodb:UpdateItem", "dynamodb:DeleteItem", "dynamodb:Query"]
        Resource = aws_dynamodb_table.users.arn
      },
      {
        Effect   = "Allow"
        Action   = ["xray:PutTraceSegments", "xray:PutTelemetryRecords"]
        Resource = "*"
      },
      {
        Effect   = "Allow"
        Action   = ["logs:CreateLogGroup", "logs:CreateLogStream", "logs:PutLogEvents"]
        Resource = "arn:aws:logs:*:*:*"
      }
    ]
  })
}

Cold Start Optimization

# Strategies to minimize cold starts:

# 1. Provisioned Concurrency — keep N environments always warm
resource "aws_lambda_provisioned_concurrency_config" "api" {
  function_name                  = aws_lambda_function.api.function_name
  qualifier                      = aws_lambda_alias.prod.name
  provisioned_concurrent_executions = 5   # always 5 warm instances
}

# 2. Keep functions small — less code = faster init
# Use Lambda layers for shared dependencies:
resource "aws_lambda_layer_version" "node_modules" {
  layer_name          = "node-dependencies"
  filename            = "layer.zip"
  compatible_runtimes = ["nodejs20.x"]
}

# 3. Use arm64 (Graviton) — 20% cheaper + often faster init
resource "aws_lambda_function" "api" {
  architectures = ["arm64"]
  # ...
}

# 4. Avoid VPC if possible — VPC cold starts add 500ms-10s
# If VPC is needed, use Hyperplane ENI (automatic in recent Lambda)

# 5. Initialize SDK clients OUTSIDE handler
# WRONG:
exports.handler = async (event) => {
  const client = new DynamoDBClient();  // created every invocation
};
# RIGHT:
const client = new DynamoDBClient();   // created once, reused
exports.handler = async (event) => { /* use client */ };

Interview Questions — Serverless

What is a Lambda cold start and how do you mitigate it?
A cold start occurs when Lambda creates a new execution environment to handle an invocation — downloading code, initializing the runtime, and running init code outside the handler. Adds 100ms–3s latency. Mitigations: 1) Provisioned Concurrency — keeps N environments pre-warmed (cost: you pay for idle time). 2) Keep function package size small — use layers, bundle only what's needed. 3) Choose arm64 — Graviton initializes faster. 4) Use SnapStart (Java) — snapshots initialized environment. 5) Move heavy initialization outside handler (SDK clients, DB connections). 6) Avoid VPC if not needed. Accept cold starts for async workloads; mitigate for latency-sensitive user-facing APIs.
When would you choose Lambda over ECS/EKS?
Choose Lambda when: short-lived tasks (<15 min), event-driven (S3 trigger, SQS consumer), infrequent/bursty traffic (pay only for execution), no state needed between invocations, rapid scaling to thousands of concurrent requests. Choose ECS/EKS when: long-running processes, persistent connections (WebSockets), complex microservices with sidecar patterns, need more control over runtime, container images already exist, latency-sensitive (no cold starts), need >10GB memory or >6 vCPU.
🚢

O. AWS ECS & Fargate — Managed Containers

Intermediate → Advanced
ECS (Elastic Container Service) is AWS's native container orchestrator. Fargate is ECS's serverless compute engine — you define the container, AWS handles the underlying EC2 instances. No node management at all.

ECS vs EKS vs Lambda — When to Use What

FactorECS + FargateEKS (Kubernetes)Lambda
Learning curveLow — AWS-nativeHigh — K8s complexityVery low
AWS integrationNative (IAM, ALB, CloudWatch)Good (IRSA, ALB controller)Native
Multi-cloudNoYes (K8s is standard)No
Node managementNone (Fargate)Required (unless Fargate mode)None
Max task durationUnlimitedUnlimited15 minutes
Cost (idle)Pay per task vCPU+memory$0.10/hr cluster + nodesZero
Best forAWS-only shops, simpler appsComplex microservices, multi-cloudEvent-driven, short tasks

ECS Core Concepts

ECS Concepts: Cluster → logical group of tasks/services (EC2 or Fargate compute) Task Definition → blueprint (like a Dockerfile + docker-compose combined) specifies: image, CPU, memory, env vars, volumes, IAM role Task → running instance of a task definition (like a pod) Service → keeps N tasks running, integrates with ALB, handles rolling updates Container → one or more containers per task (like a K8s pod)

Task Definition — Complete Example

# task-definition.json
{
  "family": "myapp-api",
  "networkMode": "awsvpc",
  "requiresCompatibilities": ["FARGATE"],
  "cpu": "512",
  "memory": "1024",
  "executionRoleArn": "arn:aws:iam::123456789:role/ecsTaskExecutionRole",
  "taskRoleArn":      "arn:aws:iam::123456789:role/myapp-task-role",
  "containerDefinitions": [
    {
      "name": "api",
      "image": "ghcr.io/myorg/api:1.5.0",
      "portMappings": [
        { "containerPort": 3000, "protocol": "tcp" }
      ],
      "environment": [
        { "name": "NODE_ENV", "value": "production" }
      ],
      "secrets": [
        {
          "name": "DB_PASSWORD",
          "valueFrom": "arn:aws:secretsmanager:us-east-1:123456789:secret:prod/db-password"
        }
      ],
      "logConfiguration": {
        "logDriver": "awslogs",
        "options": {
          "awslogs-group": "/ecs/myapp-api",
          "awslogs-region": "us-east-1",
          "awslogs-stream-prefix": "ecs"
        }
      },
      "healthCheck": {
        "command": ["CMD-SHELL", "curl -f http://localhost:3000/health || exit 1"],
        "interval": 30,
        "timeout": 5,
        "retries": 3,
        "startPeriod": 60
      },
      "readonlyRootFilesystem": true,
      "user": "1000",
      "essential": true
    }
  ]
}

ECS Service + ALB with Terraform

# ECS Cluster
resource "aws_ecs_cluster" "main" {
  name = "${var.project}-${var.environment}"

  setting {
    name  = "containerInsights"
    value = "enabled"   # CloudWatch Container Insights metrics
  }
}

resource "aws_ecs_cluster_capacity_providers" "main" {
  cluster_name       = aws_ecs_cluster.main.name
  capacity_providers = ["FARGATE", "FARGATE_SPOT"]

  default_capacity_provider_strategy {
    base              = 1
    weight            = 40
    capacity_provider = "FARGATE"        # 40% on-demand
  }
  default_capacity_provider_strategy {
    weight            = 60
    capacity_provider = "FARGATE_SPOT"   # 60% Spot (70% cheaper)
  }
}

# Task Definition (from JSON file)
resource "aws_ecs_task_definition" "api" {
  family                   = "${var.project}-api"
  requires_compatibilities = ["FARGATE"]
  network_mode             = "awsvpc"
  cpu                      = 512
  memory                   = 1024
  execution_role_arn       = aws_iam_role.ecs_execution.arn
  task_role_arn            = aws_iam_role.ecs_task.arn
  container_definitions    = templatefile("${path.module}/task-definition.json.tpl", {
    image      = "${var.ecr_url}/api:${var.image_tag}"
    db_secret  = aws_secretsmanager_secret.db.arn
    log_group  = aws_cloudwatch_log_group.ecs.name
    region     = var.aws_region
  })
}

# ECS Service
resource "aws_ecs_service" "api" {
  name                               = "${var.project}-api"
  cluster                            = aws_ecs_cluster.main.id
  task_definition                    = aws_ecs_task_definition.api.arn
  desired_count                      = var.desired_count
  health_check_grace_period_seconds  = 60

  capacity_provider_strategy {
    capacity_provider = "FARGATE"
    weight            = 40
    base              = 1
  }
  capacity_provider_strategy {
    capacity_provider = "FARGATE_SPOT"
    weight            = 60
  }

  network_configuration {
    subnets          = module.vpc.private_subnet_ids
    security_groups  = [aws_security_group.ecs_tasks.id]
    assign_public_ip = false
  }

  load_balancer {
    target_group_arn = aws_lb_target_group.api.arn
    container_name   = "api"
    container_port   = 3000
  }

  deployment_circuit_breaker {
    enable   = true
    rollback = true    # auto-rollback on failed deploy
  }

  deployment_controller { type = "ECS" }   # or "CODE_DEPLOY" for blue-green
}

# Auto Scaling
resource "aws_appautoscaling_target" "ecs" {
  max_capacity       = 20
  min_capacity       = 2
  resource_id        = "service/${aws_ecs_cluster.main.name}/${aws_ecs_service.api.name}"
  scalable_dimension = "ecs:service:DesiredCount"
  service_namespace  = "ecs"
}

resource "aws_appautoscaling_policy" "cpu" {
  name               = "${var.project}-cpu-scaling"
  policy_type        = "TargetTrackingScaling"
  resource_id        = aws_appautoscaling_target.ecs.resource_id
  scalable_dimension = aws_appautoscaling_target.ecs.scalable_dimension
  service_namespace  = aws_appautoscaling_target.ecs.service_namespace

  target_tracking_scaling_policy_configuration {
    predefined_metric_specification {
      predefined_metric_type = "ECSServiceAverageCPUUtilization"
    }
    target_value       = 60.0
    scale_in_cooldown  = 300
    scale_out_cooldown = 60
  }
}

ECR — Elastic Container Registry

# Create ECR repository
aws ecr create-repository \
  --repository-name myapp/api \
  --image-scanning-configuration scanOnPush=true \
  --encryption-configuration encryptionType=AES256

# Login and push
aws ecr get-login-password --region us-east-1 | \
  docker login --username AWS --password-stdin \
  123456789.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com

docker tag myapp/api:latest \
  123456789.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp/api:latest

docker push 123456789.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp/api:latest

# ECR lifecycle policy — keep only last 10 images
aws ecr put-lifecycle-policy \
  --repository-name myapp/api \
  --lifecycle-policy-text '{
    "rules": [{
      "rulePriority": 1,
      "description": "Keep last 10 images",
      "selection": {
        "tagStatus": "any",
        "countType": "imageCountMoreThan",
        "countNumber": 10
      },
      "action": { "type": "expire" }
    }]
  }'

Interview Questions — ECS

An ECS Fargate task keeps failing to start. How do you debug it?
1) Check ECS service events: aws ecs describe-services --cluster myapp --services api — look at Events array. 2) Check stopped task details: aws ecs describe-tasks --cluster myapp --tasks <task-arn> — stoppedReason field. 3) Common causes: image pull failure (ECR permissions, wrong tag), health check failing (app not starting in time — increase startPeriod), secrets access failure (task role missing Secrets Manager permission), out of memory (increase memory in task definition), subnet/SG misconfiguration (task can't reach internet for image pull). 4) Check CloudWatch Logs: /ecs/myapp-api log group for container stdout/stderr.
☸️

P. Kubernetes Advanced — StatefulSets, Storage, Scheduling

Advanced

StatefulSets — For Stateful Applications

# PostgreSQL StatefulSet
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: StatefulSet
metadata:
  name: postgres
  namespace: production
spec:
  serviceName: postgres-headless   # required — governs pod DNS
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: postgres
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: postgres
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: postgres
        image: postgres:16-alpine
        ports:
        - containerPort: 5432
        env:
        - name: POSTGRES_DB
          value: appdb
        - name: POSTGRES_PASSWORD
          valueFrom:
            secretKeyRef:
              name: postgres-secret
              key: password
        - name: PGDATA
          value: /var/lib/postgresql/data/pgdata
        resources:
          requests: { memory: "512Mi", cpu: "250m" }
          limits:   { memory: "2Gi",   cpu: "1000m" }
        volumeMounts:
        - name: postgres-data
          mountPath: /var/lib/postgresql/data
        readinessProbe:
          exec:
            command: ["pg_isready", "-U", "postgres"]
          initialDelaySeconds: 15
          periodSeconds: 5
  volumeClaimTemplates:   # creates PVC per pod: postgres-data-postgres-0, -1, -2
  - metadata:
      name: postgres-data
    spec:
      accessModes: ["ReadWriteOnce"]
      storageClassName: gp3
      resources:
        requests:
          storage: 100Gi
---
# Headless Service — stable DNS: postgres-0.postgres-headless.production.svc.cluster.local
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: postgres-headless
  namespace: production
spec:
  clusterIP: None     # headless — no virtual IP, DNS returns pod IPs directly
  selector:
    app: postgres
  ports:
  - port: 5432
---
# Regular Service — for app to connect (routes to primary)
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: postgres
  namespace: production
spec:
  selector:
    app: postgres
  ports:
  - port: 5432

DaemonSets — One Pod Per Node

# Use cases: log collectors, monitoring agents, network plugins, security agents
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: DaemonSet
metadata:
  name: filebeat
  namespace: monitoring
spec:
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: filebeat
  updateStrategy:
    type: RollingUpdate
    rollingUpdate:
      maxUnavailable: 1
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: filebeat
    spec:
      tolerations:
      - key: node-role.kubernetes.io/control-plane   # run on master nodes too
        effect: NoSchedule
      containers:
      - name: filebeat
        image: docker.elastic.co/beats/filebeat:8.11.0
        resources:
          requests: { memory: "100Mi", cpu: "100m" }
          limits:   { memory: "200Mi", cpu: "200m" }
        volumeMounts:
        - name: varlog
          mountPath: /var/log
          readOnly: true
        - name: varlibdockercontainers
          mountPath: /var/lib/docker/containers
          readOnly: true
      volumes:
      - name: varlog
        hostPath:
          path: /var/log
      - name: varlibdockercontainers
        hostPath:
          path: /var/lib/docker/containers

Jobs & CronJobs

# One-time Job — database migration
apiVersion: batch/v1
kind: Job
metadata:
  name: db-migrate-v1-5
  namespace: production
spec:
  backoffLimit: 3
  activeDeadlineSeconds: 300   # fail if not done in 5 min
  template:
    spec:
      restartPolicy: OnFailure
      containers:
      - name: migrate
        image: myorg/api:1.5.0
        command: ["npm", "run", "migrate"]
        env:
        - name: DATABASE_URL
          valueFrom:
            secretKeyRef:
              name: db-secret
              key: url

---
# CronJob — nightly data export at 2AM UTC
apiVersion: batch/v1
kind: CronJob
metadata:
  name: nightly-export
  namespace: production
spec:
  schedule: "0 2 * * *"
  concurrencyPolicy: Forbid       # don't run if previous still running
  successfulJobsHistoryLimit: 3
  failedJobsHistoryLimit: 3
  startingDeadlineSeconds: 600    # give up if 10 min late
  jobTemplate:
    spec:
      template:
        spec:
          restartPolicy: OnFailure
          containers:
          - name: exporter
            image: myorg/exporter:latest
            resources:
              requests: { memory: "256Mi", cpu: "100m" }

Persistent Volumes — Storage Deep Dive

# StorageClass — defines the type of storage
apiVersion: storage.k8s.io/v1
kind: StorageClass
metadata:
  name: gp3
  annotations:
    storageclass.kubernetes.io/is-default-class: "true"
provisioner: ebs.csi.aws.com
parameters:
  type: gp3
  iopsPerGB: "3000"
  throughput: "125"
  encrypted: "true"
reclaimPolicy: Retain          # don't delete PV when PVC deleted
allowVolumeExpansion: true
volumeBindingMode: WaitForFirstConsumer  # provision in same AZ as pod

---
# PersistentVolumeClaim
apiVersion: v1
kind: PersistentVolumeClaim
metadata:
  name: app-data
  namespace: production
spec:
  accessModes: [ReadWriteOnce]   # RWO: one node; RWX: many nodes (EFS)
  storageClassName: gp3
  resources:
    requests:
      storage: 50Gi

---
# Pod using PVC
spec:
  containers:
  - name: app
    volumeMounts:
    - name: data
      mountPath: /data
  volumes:
  - name: data
    persistentVolumeClaim:
      claimName: app-data

Advanced Scheduling — Taints, Tolerations & Affinity

# Taint a node (mark it as special — no pods unless they tolerate)
kubectl taint nodes gpu-node-1 gpu=true:NoSchedule
kubectl taint nodes spot-node-1 spot=true:PreferNoSchedule

# Toleration in Pod spec — "I can run on gpu nodes"
spec:
  tolerations:
  - key: "gpu"
    operator: "Equal"
    value: "true"
    effect: "NoSchedule"

# Node Affinity — schedule on specific nodes
spec:
  affinity:
    nodeAffinity:
      requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:   # hard rule
        nodeSelectorTerms:
        - matchExpressions:
          - key: node.kubernetes.io/instance-type
            operator: In
            values: ["m6i.xlarge", "m6i.2xlarge"]
      preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:  # soft rule
      - weight: 80
        preference:
          matchExpressions:
          - key: topology.kubernetes.io/zone
            operator: In
            values: ["us-east-1a"]

# Pod Anti-Affinity — spread across nodes/AZs
spec:
  affinity:
    podAntiAffinity:
      requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
      - labelSelector:
          matchLabels:
            app: api
        topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname   # one pod per node

Resource Quotas & LimitRange

# ResourceQuota — limit total resources in namespace
apiVersion: v1
kind: ResourceQuota
metadata:
  name: production-quota
  namespace: production
spec:
  hard:
    requests.cpu: "20"
    requests.memory: 40Gi
    limits.cpu: "40"
    limits.memory: 80Gi
    pods: "100"
    services: "20"
    persistentvolumeclaims: "20"

---
# LimitRange — default + max per container
apiVersion: v1
kind: LimitRange
metadata:
  name: container-limits
  namespace: production
spec:
  limits:
  - type: Container
    default:          # applied if no limits specified
      cpu: 200m
      memory: 256Mi
    defaultRequest:
      cpu: 100m
      memory: 128Mi
    max:             # hard ceiling
      cpu: "4"
      memory: 8Gi

Interview Questions — K8s Advanced

When would you use a StatefulSet vs a Deployment?
Use StatefulSet when: 1) Each pod needs stable, unique network identity (postgres-0, postgres-1 DNS names). 2) Each pod needs its own persistent storage (volumeClaimTemplates creates a PVC per pod — deleting pod-0 doesn't delete its PVC). 3) Ordered startup/shutdown matters (pod-1 only starts after pod-0 is Ready). 4) Examples: PostgreSQL, MySQL, Cassandra, Kafka, ZooKeeper, Redis cluster. Use Deployment when: pods are interchangeable, storage is shared or ephemeral, order doesn't matter. Key gotcha: StatefulSet scale-down removes the highest-numbered pod first (pod-2 before pod-1 before pod-0) — important for Kafka partition leaders.
Explain PersistentVolume, PersistentVolumeClaim, and StorageClass. How do they relate?
StorageClass: Defines the type of storage and provisioner (e.g., AWS EBS gp3 with EBS CSI driver). Acts as a template.
PersistentVolume (PV): Actual storage resource in the cluster — either manually created by admin (static provisioning) or automatically created by the provisioner when a PVC is made (dynamic provisioning).
PersistentVolumeClaim (PVC): Pod's request for storage — "I need 50Gi ReadWriteOnce". Kubernetes binds it to a matching PV. The pod uses the PVC; it doesn't care about the underlying storage type. This abstraction means you can switch from local SSD to EBS without changing pod specs.
🕸️

Q. Service Mesh — Istio

Senior Level
Real-world analogy: Istio is like a telecommunications network's management plane — all calls (requests) between services go through it, giving you observability, traffic control, and security without changing any application code.

What Istio Adds to Kubernetes

FeatureWithout IstioWith Istio
mTLS between servicesManual cert management in each serviceAutomatic — Envoy sidecars handle it
Traffic splittingCustom code or Ingress annotationsVirtualService with weight: 90/10
Retries & timeoutsCode in every serviceVirtualService policy — no code change
ObservabilityEach service instruments itselfAutomatic metrics, traces per request
Circuit breakingLibrary per service (Hystrix)DestinationRule outlierDetection

Istio Architecture

Data Plane: Envoy proxies (sidecars) auto-injected into every pod Control Plane: Istiod (Pilot + Citadel + Galley merged) [App Container] + [Envoy Sidecar] ← all traffic goes through Envoy ↕ [Istiod] — pushes config (routes, certs, policies) to all Envoys ↕ [App Container] + [Envoy Sidecar] Request flow: Pod-A Envoy → (mTLS) → Pod-B Envoy → Pod-B App

Canary Deployment with VirtualService

# Deploy v2 alongside v1
kubectl set image deployment/api api=myorg/api:v2 --record=false
# But don't route traffic to it yet — use Istio to control traffic

---
apiVersion: networking.istio.io/v1beta1
kind: VirtualService
metadata:
  name: api-vs
  namespace: production
spec:
  hosts:
  - api
  http:
  - match:
    - headers:
        x-canary:
          exact: "true"
    route:
    - destination:
        host: api
        subset: v2
  - route:                    # default — 95% v1, 5% v2
    - destination:
        host: api
        subset: v1
      weight: 95
    - destination:
        host: api
        subset: v2
      weight: 5
    retries:
      attempts: 3
      perTryTimeout: 5s
    timeout: 15s

---
apiVersion: networking.istio.io/v1beta1
kind: DestinationRule
metadata:
  name: api-dr
  namespace: production
spec:
  host: api
  trafficPolicy:
    connectionPool:
      http:
        h2UpgradePolicy: UPGRADE
    outlierDetection:         # circuit breaker
      consecutiveErrors: 5
      interval: 30s
      baseEjectionTime: 30s
      maxEjectionPercent: 50
  subsets:
  - name: v1
    labels:
      version: v1
  - name: v2
    labels:
      version: v2

Mutual TLS (mTLS)

# Enable strict mTLS for entire namespace
apiVersion: security.istio.io/v1beta1
kind: PeerAuthentication
metadata:
  name: default
  namespace: production
spec:
  mtls:
    mode: STRICT   # all service-to-service traffic must use mTLS

---
# AuthorizationPolicy — who can call what
apiVersion: security.istio.io/v1beta1
kind: AuthorizationPolicy
metadata:
  name: api-authz
  namespace: production
spec:
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: api
  action: ALLOW
  rules:
  - from:
    - source:
        principals: ["cluster.local/ns/production/sa/frontend-sa"]
  - to:
    - operation:
        methods: ["GET", "POST"]
        paths: ["/api/*"]

Interview Questions — Service Mesh

What problems does a service mesh solve that you can't solve with K8s alone?
Kubernetes handles service discovery and load balancing but gives you no observability into inter-service traffic, no traffic control beyond basic LB, no security between services, and no circuit breaking. Service mesh (Istio) adds: 1) mTLS encryption + authentication between every pair of services — without any code change. 2) Fine-grained traffic control (canary %, header-based routing, retries, timeouts) at the infrastructure level. 3) Automatic distributed tracing (every request tagged with trace ID, propagated via headers). 4) Per-service request metrics (Prometheus) automatically. 5) Circuit breaking and outlier detection. The key: all this is configuration (CRDs), not application code change.
📨

R. Message Queues — Kafka, SQS, RabbitMQ

Intermediate → Advanced
Real-world analogy: Message queues are like a post office — the sender drops a letter (message) and moves on. The recipient picks it up when ready. Neither needs to be online at the same time — fully decoupled.

When to Use Message Queues

  • Decoupling: Order service publishes "order.created" — inventory, email, billing each consume independently
  • Load leveling: Spike of 10,000 requests → queue absorbs burst → workers process at their own pace
  • Async processing: User uploads video → API returns immediately → worker processes video in background
  • Reliability: If consumer crashes mid-processing, message stays in queue and gets retried
  • Fan-out: One event → multiple consumers (SNS → SQS queues)

Kafka vs SQS vs RabbitMQ

FeatureApache KafkaAWS SQSRabbitMQ
TypeLog-based distributed streamingManaged queue (cloud-native)Message broker (AMQP)
Message retentionDays/weeks (configurable)Up to 14 daysUntil consumed
ReplayYes — consumers can re-read old messagesNoNo
OrderingPer-partition orderingFIFO queue optionPer-queue ordering
ThroughputMillions/secThousands/secTens of thousands/sec
SetupComplex (brokers, ZooKeeper/KRaft)Zero (fully managed)Moderate
Best forEvent sourcing, analytics pipelines, audit logsSimple task queues, AWS-nativeComplex routing, multiple exchanges

AWS SQS — Complete Example

# Create SQS queue with DLQ (Dead Letter Queue)
# Terraform
resource "aws_sqs_queue" "main" {
  name                       = "${var.project}-orders"
  visibility_timeout_seconds = 300   # how long consumer has to process
  message_retention_seconds  = 86400 # 1 day
  receive_wait_time_seconds  = 20    # long polling — cheaper
  max_message_size           = 262144

  redrive_policy = jsonencode({
    deadLetterTargetArn = aws_sqs_queue.dlq.arn
    maxReceiveCount     = 3           # move to DLQ after 3 failed attempts
  })

  tags = { Name = "${var.project}-orders" }
}

resource "aws_sqs_queue" "dlq" {
  name                      = "${var.project}-orders-dlq"
  message_retention_seconds = 1209600   # 14 days — time to investigate
}

# Lambda consumer — triggered by SQS
resource "aws_lambda_event_source_mapping" "sqs" {
  event_source_arn = aws_sqs_queue.main.arn
  function_name    = aws_lambda_function.order_processor.arn
  batch_size       = 10
  function_response_types = ["ReportBatchItemFailures"]  # partial batch failure
}
// SQS consumer Lambda handler
exports.handler = async (event) => {
  const failures = [];

  await Promise.all(event.Records.map(async (record) => {
    try {
      const body = JSON.parse(record.body);
      await processOrder(body);
      console.log('Processed order:', body.orderId);
    } catch (err) {
      console.error('Failed to process:', record.messageId, err);
      failures.push({ itemIdentifier: record.messageId }); // partial failure
    }
  }));

  // Only failed messages go back to queue; successful ones are deleted
  return { batchItemFailures: failures };
};

  --replication-factor 1
