Gateway + VirtualService (Production)

# istio-ingress.yaml
apiVersion: networking.istio.io/v1beta1
kind: Gateway
metadata:
  name: prod-gateway
  namespace: istio-system
spec:
  selector:
    istio: ingressgateway
  servers:
    - port:
        number: 443
        name: https
        protocol: HTTPS
      tls:
        mode: SIMPLE
        credentialName: prod-tls-secret    # cert-manager secret
      hosts: ["api.myapp.com","*.myapp.com"]
    - port:
        number: 80
        name: http
        protocol: HTTP
      hosts: ["*"]
      tls:
        httpsRedirect: true
---
apiVersion: networking.istio.io/v1beta1
kind: VirtualService
metadata:
  name: api-vs
spec:
  hosts: ["api.myapp.com"]
  gateways: ["istio-system/prod-gateway"]
  http:
    # Header-based canary routing
    - match:
        - headers:
            x-canary: { exact: "true" }
      route:
        - destination:
            host: api-service
            subset: canary
    # A/B test by user segment
    - match:
        - headers:
            x-user-segment: { regex: "beta-.*" }
      route:
        - destination:
            host: api-service
            subset: v2
    # Production traffic split (canary rollout)
    - route:
        - destination:
            host: api-service
            subset: stable
          weight: 90
        - destination:
            host: api-service
            subset: canary
          weight: 10
      timeout: 10s
      retries:
        attempts: 3
        perTryTimeout: 3s
        retryOn: "5xx,reset,connect-failure,retriable-4xx"
---
apiVersion: networking.istio.io/v1beta1
kind: DestinationRule
metadata:
  name: api-dr
spec:
  host: api-service
  trafficPolicy:
    connectionPool:
      tcp:
        maxConnections: 1000
        connectTimeout: 5s
      http:
        http1MaxPendingRequests: 100
        http2MaxRequests: 1000
        maxRequestsPerConnection: 10
    outlierDetection:
      consecutiveGatewayErrors: 5
      interval: 30s
      baseEjectionTime: 30s
      maxEjectionPercent: 50
  subsets:
    - name: stable
      labels: { version: stable }
    - name: canary
      labels: { version: canary }
    - name: v2
      labels: { version: v2 }

Flagger Canary (Automated Progressive Delivery)

# Install Flagger for Istio
helm upgrade -i flagger flagger/flagger \
  --namespace=istio-system \
  --set crd.create=false \
  --set meshProvider=istio \
  --set metricsServer=http://prometheus:9090
# canary.yaml — Automated canary with Flagger
apiVersion: flagger.app/v1beta1
kind: Canary
metadata:
  name: api-service
  namespace: default
spec:
  targetRef:
    apiVersion: apps/v1
    kind: Deployment
    name: api-service
  progressDeadlineSeconds: 600
  service:
    port: 80
    targetPort: 8080
    gateways: ["istio-system/prod-gateway"]
    hosts: ["api.myapp.com"]
    retries:
      attempts: 3
      perTryTimeout: 5s
      retryOn: "gateway-error,connect-failure,refused-stream"
  analysis:
    interval: 1m
    threshold: 5              # Max failed checks before rollback
    maxWeight: 50             # Max canary traffic (50%)
    stepWeight: 10            # Increment per check
    metrics:
      - name: request-success-rate
        thresholdRange: { min: 99 }
        interval: 1m
      - name: request-duration
        thresholdRange: { max: 500 }   # p99 < 500ms
        interval: 1m
    webhooks:
      - name: load-test
        url: http://flagger-loadtester.test/
        timeout: 5s
        metadata:
          cmd: "hey -z 1m -q 10 -c 2 http://api-service-canary.default/"
      - name: slack-notify
        url: https://hooks.slack.com/services/XXX
        type: confirm
        metadata:
          message: "Canary analysis for {{ name }} passed! Promote to production?"

mTLS Policy + Authorization

# security.yaml
apiVersion: security.istio.io/v1beta1
kind: PeerAuthentication
metadata:
  name: default
  namespace: default
spec:
  mtls:
    mode: STRICT        # All pod-to-pod traffic must use mTLS
---
apiVersion: security.istio.io/v1beta1
kind: AuthorizationPolicy
metadata:
  name: api-authz
  namespace: default
spec:
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: api-service
  action: ALLOW
  rules:
    # Only allow frontend and internal services
    - from:
        - source:
            principals:
              - cluster.local/ns/default/sa/frontend
              - cluster.local/ns/monitoring/sa/prometheus
      to:
        - operation:
            methods: ["GET"]
            paths: ["/api/*", "/health", "/metrics"]
    # Admin endpoints require specific service account
    - from:
        - source:
            principals: ["cluster.local/ns/default/sa/admin-service"]
      to:
        - operation:
            methods: ["GET","POST","PUT","DELETE"]
            paths: ["/admin/*"]
Q: What is the difference between Istio's Circuit Breaker and Kubernetes Liveness Probes?
A: They operate at different layers. Kubernetes Liveness Probes restart individual pods when they become unhealthy — they act on the container level. Istio's Circuit Breaker (via outlierDetection in DestinationRule) operates at the traffic routing layer — it temporarily ejects upstream hosts from the load balancing pool when they return consecutive errors, stopping traffic to degraded instances without killing them. Istio CB responds to actual error rates in real traffic, while K8s probes use synthetic checks. Together: K8s probes handle permanent failures, Istio handles transient service-level degradation.

Advanced CI/CD Patterns

GitOps with ArgoCD App-of-Apps

# apps/root-app.yaml — Root Application
apiVersion: argoproj.io/v1alpha1
kind: Application
metadata:
  name: root-app
  namespace: argocd
  finalizers: [resources-finalizer.argocd.argoproj.io]
spec:
  project: default
  source:
    repoURL: https://github.com/org/gitops-repo
    targetRevision: HEAD
    path: apps/
  destination:
    server: https://kubernetes.default.svc
    namespace: argocd
  syncPolicy:
    automated:
      prune: true
      selfHeal: true
    syncOptions: [CreateNamespace=true]
---
# apps/myapp.yaml — Child Application
apiVersion: argoproj.io/v1alpha1
kind: Application
metadata:
  name: myapp-prod
  namespace: argocd
spec:
  project: prod
  source:
    repoURL: https://github.com/org/gitops-repo
    targetRevision: HEAD
    path: environments/prod/myapp
    helm:
      valueFiles: [values-prod.yaml]
      parameters:
        - name: image.tag
          value: "1.2.3"
  destination:
    server: https://eks-prod.example.com
    namespace: myapp
  syncPolicy:
    automated:
      prune: true
      selfHeal: true
    retry:
      limit: 5
      backoff: { duration: 5s, factor: 2, maxDuration: 3m }
  revisionHistoryLimit: 10

Blue-Green Deployment with Kubernetes

# blue-green strategy using service selector switching
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: myapp-blue
  labels:
    app: myapp
    slot: blue
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels: { app: myapp, slot: blue }
  template:
    metadata:
      labels: { app: myapp, slot: blue, version: v1 }
    spec:
      containers:
        - name: myapp
          image: myapp:v1
---
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: myapp-green
  labels:
    app: myapp
    slot: green
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels: { app: myapp, slot: green }
  template:
    metadata:
      labels: { app: myapp, slot: green, version: v2 }
    spec:
      containers:
        - name: myapp
          image: myapp:v2
---
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: myapp
spec:
  selector:
    app: myapp
    slot: blue     # Switch to 'green' to cut over
  ports:
    - port: 80
      targetPort: 8080
# Blue-Green switch script
CURRENT=$(kubectl get service myapp -o jsonpath='{.spec.selector.slot}')
NEW=$([ "$CURRENT" = "blue" ] && echo "green" || echo "blue")

# Verify new slot is healthy first
kubectl rollout status deployment/myapp-$NEW --timeout=300s

# Switch traffic
kubectl patch service myapp -p "{\"spec\":{\"selector\":{\"slot\":\"$NEW\"}}}"
echo "Switched from $CURRENT to $NEW"

# Keep old deployment for quick rollback (delete after confidence)
# kubectl delete deployment myapp-$CURRENT

GitHub Actions — Reusable Workflows

# .github/workflows/reusable-deploy.yml
name: Reusable Deploy
on:
  workflow_call:
    inputs:
      environment:
        required: true
        type: string
      image_tag:
        required: true
        type: string
      service_name:
        required: true
        type: string
    secrets:
      AWS_ROLE:
        required: true

jobs:
  deploy:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    environment: ${{ inputs.environment }}
    permissions:
      id-token: write
    steps:
      - uses: aws-actions/configure-aws-credentials@v4
        with:
          role-to-assume: ${{ secrets.AWS_ROLE }}
          aws-region: us-east-1
      - name: Deploy to ECS
        run: |
          aws ecs update-service \
            --cluster ${{ inputs.environment }}-cluster \
            --service ${{ inputs.service_name }} \
            --force-new-deployment

# .github/workflows/main.yml — Caller
name: CI/CD
on:
  push:
    branches: [main]
jobs:
  build:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    outputs:
      image_tag: ${{ steps.meta.outputs.version }}
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - id: meta
        uses: docker/metadata-action@v5
        with:
          images: ${{ secrets.ECR_REGISTRY }}/myapp
          tags: type=sha

  deploy-staging:
    needs: build
    uses: ./.github/workflows/reusable-deploy.yml
    with:
      environment: staging
      image_tag: ${{ needs.build.outputs.image_tag }}
      service_name: myapp
    secrets:
      AWS_ROLE: ${{ secrets.STAGING_DEPLOY_ROLE }}

  deploy-prod:
    needs: deploy-staging
    uses: ./.github/workflows/reusable-deploy.yml
    with:
      environment: production
      image_tag: ${{ needs.build.outputs.image_tag }}
      service_name: myapp
    secrets:
      AWS_ROLE: ${{ secrets.PROD_DEPLOY_ROLE }}
Q: When would you choose Blue-Green over Canary deployment?
A: Blue-Green is best when you need instant rollback (one service selector change), for stateful apps where gradual migration would cause session split, or when the change is a schema migration requiring a full cutover. Canary is better when you want to validate performance on real traffic progressively, when you have sufficient metrics to automate promotion/rollback decisions (like Flagger), or when the change is risky enough to warrant gradual exposure. Blue-Green has higher resource cost (2x infra during switch); Canary has higher operational complexity.

Kubernetes Security Hardening

Pod Security Standards

# Enforce restricted PSS at namespace level
apiVersion: v1
kind: Namespace
metadata:
  name: prod
  labels:
    pod-security.kubernetes.io/enforce: restricted
    pod-security.kubernetes.io/audit: restricted
    pod-security.kubernetes.io/warn: restricted
---
# Compliant pod spec
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: secure-pod
  namespace: prod
spec:
  securityContext:
    runAsNonRoot: true
    runAsUser: 1000
    runAsGroup: 3000
    fsGroup: 2000
    seccompProfile:
      type: RuntimeDefault
  containers:
    - name: app
      image: myapp:1.0
      securityContext:
        allowPrivilegeEscalation: false
        readOnlyRootFilesystem: true
        capabilities:
          drop: ["ALL"]
      resources:
        requests: { cpu: 100m, memory: 128Mi }
        limits:   { cpu: 500m, memory: 512Mi }
      volumeMounts:
        - name: tmp
          mountPath: /tmp
        - name: cache
          mountPath: /app/cache
  volumes:
    - name: tmp
      emptyDir: {}
    - name: cache
      emptyDir: {}

OPA Gatekeeper Policies

# Require all images to come from approved registry
apiVersion: templates.gatekeeper.sh/v1
kind: ConstraintTemplate
metadata:
  name: allowedregistries
spec:
  crd:
    spec:
      names: { kind: AllowedRegistries }
      validation:
        openAPIV3Schema:
          properties:
            registries: { type: array, items: { type: string } }
  targets:
    - target: admission.k8s.gatekeeper.sh
      rego: |
        package allowedregistries
        violation[{"msg": msg}] {
          container := input.review.object.spec.containers[_]
          not startswith_any(container.image, input.parameters.registries)
          msg := sprintf("Image '%v' not from approved registry", [container.image])
        }
        startswith_any(str, prefixes) {
          startswith(str, prefixes[_])
        }
---
apiVersion: constraints.gatekeeper.sh/v1beta1
kind: AllowedRegistries
metadata:
  name: prod-registries
spec:
  match:
    kinds: [{ apiGroups: [""], kinds: ["Pod"] }]
    namespaces: ["prod", "staging"]
  parameters:
    registries:
      - "123456789.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/"
      - "gcr.io/myproject/"

RBAC Best Practices

# Principle of least privilege
apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
kind: Role
metadata:
  name: app-role
  namespace: prod
rules:
  - apiGroups: [""]
    resources: ["configmaps"]
    verbs: ["get","list","watch"]
  - apiGroups: [""]
    resources: ["secrets"]
    resourceNames: ["myapp-secret"]   # Named secret only
    verbs: ["get"]
---
apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
kind: RoleBinding
metadata:
  name: app-rolebinding
  namespace: prod
subjects:
  - kind: ServiceAccount
    name: myapp-sa
    namespace: prod
roleRef:
  kind: Role
  name: app-role
  apiGroup: rbac.authorization.k8s.io
---
# IRSA — IAM Roles for Service Accounts (EKS)
apiVersion: v1
kind: ServiceAccount
metadata:
  name: myapp-sa
  namespace: prod
  annotations:
    eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn: arn:aws:iam::123456789:role/myapp-eks-role

Network Policies — Zero Trust

# Default deny all ingress and egress
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: default-deny-all
  namespace: prod
spec:
  podSelector: {}
  policyTypes: [Ingress, Egress]
---
# Allow specific communication
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: allow-api-to-db
  namespace: prod
spec:
  podSelector:
    matchLabels: { app: postgres }
  policyTypes: [Ingress]
  ingress:
    - from:
        - podSelector:
            matchLabels: { app: api-service }
      ports:
        - protocol: TCP
          port: 5432
---
# Allow monitoring scrape
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: allow-prometheus
  namespace: prod
spec:
  podSelector: {}
  policyTypes: [Ingress]
  ingress:
    - from:
        - namespaceSelector:
            matchLabels: { kubernetes.io/metadata.name: monitoring }
          podSelector:
            matchLabels: { app: prometheus }
      ports:
        - protocol: TCP
          port: 9090

Advanced Interview Q&A

Q: Explain the CAP theorem and how it applies to distributed systems you've worked with.
A: CAP theorem states a distributed system can guarantee at most 2 of 3: Consistency (all nodes see same data), Availability (every request gets a response), Partition Tolerance (system works despite network splits). Since network partitions are inevitable in production, you choose CP or AP. DynamoDB defaults to AP (eventual consistency) but supports strongly consistent reads. PostgreSQL Multi-AZ is CP — during failover (~30s), writes are unavailable but consistency is preserved. Kafka is CP by design — leader election during broker failure sacrifices availability briefly. In practice, design for AP and add compensating transactions where consistency matters.
Q: How do you approach a production incident where latency has spiked 10x?
A: Follow the Four Golden Signals first: check latency (is it all endpoints or specific ones?), error rate (are errors also up?), traffic (did volume spike?), saturation (CPU/memory/connections at limit?). Then: check recent deployments with kubectl rollout history or deployment tracking. Look at APM traces (Jaeger/X-Ray) to find slow spans. Check DB slow query logs. Check connection pool exhaustion. Typical root causes: N+1 queries, missing index on new filter, connection pool saturation, downstream service degraded. If a recent deploy — rollback immediately, investigate afterward. Communicate status every 15 minutes via status page.
Q: What is the difference between horizontal and vertical scaling, and when would you use each?
A: Vertical scaling (scale up) — add more CPU/RAM to existing instances. Simple, no code changes, but has hard limits, single point of failure, and requires downtime for most instance types. Use for: databases (easier than sharding), stateful apps, when traffic is predictable and bounded. Horizontal scaling (scale out) — add more instances behind a load balancer. Unlimited scale, high availability, but requires stateless design and session management (sticky sessions or centralized session store like Redis). Use for: stateless APIs, web servers, Kubernetes pods with HPA. In practice: scale vertically first (easier), then horizontally once you hit limits. For databases: vertical + read replicas, then sharding or Aurora Serverless.
Q: How does Kubernetes handle pod scheduling and what happens when resources are insufficient?
A: The scheduler filters nodes by: resource requests (not limits) — node must have enough allocatable CPU/memory; node selectors/affinity rules; taints/tolerations; topology spread constraints. Then scores remaining nodes by resource utilization balance, image locality, etc. If no node fits, the pod enters Pending state with reason Unschedulable. Check: kubectl describe pod <pod> shows the scheduling failure reason. Solutions: scale up node group (Cluster Autoscaler fires if annotation is present), reduce resource requests, add node affinity overrides, or check if taints are blocking. Common issue: pods requesting more memory than any single node's allocatable capacity (always less than instance memory due to OS and daemon overhead).
Q: Explain how you would implement zero-downtime database schema migrations.
A: Use the expand-contract pattern across 3 deployments: 1) Expand — add new column (nullable, no default required) — old and new code both work; 2) Migrate — backfill data in batches (UPDATE ... WHERE id BETWEEN x AND y to avoid lock contention), deploy new code that writes to both old and new column; 3) Contract — drop old column once all traffic uses new schema. Never: ALTER TABLE to add NOT NULL without default on large tables (locks entire table), rename columns without backward-compatible period, drop columns that active code reads. Use Flyway/Liquibase for versioned migrations and never run DDL in transactions on large tables in PostgreSQL — use CREATE INDEX CONCURRENTLY and ADD COLUMN + separate backfill.
Q: What is your approach to secrets management in a multi-environment Kubernetes setup?
A: Never store secrets in Git, even encrypted base64. Use External Secrets Operator (ESO) to sync from AWS Secrets Manager or HashiCorp Vault into Kubernetes Secrets at runtime. ESO creates a SecretStore (credentials to access the secret backend) and ExternalSecret (what to fetch and where to put it). The actual secret values live only in Secrets Manager/Vault. For EKS: use IRSA so the ESO pod assumes an IAM role scoped to only read specific secrets. Rotate secrets automatically via Secrets Manager rotation — ESO re-syncs on next reconciliation (configurable interval). Seal Secrets (Bitnami) is an alternative for GitOps-friendly encrypted secrets, but requires your own key management.
Q: How do you handle multi-region disaster recovery with RTO/RPO requirements?
A: Define targets first: RPO (max data loss) and RTO (max downtime). For RPO=0 (no data loss): active-active multi-region with synchronous replication — complex, expensive. For RPO < 1min: Aurora Global Database with async cross-region replication (typically <1s lag). For RTO=0: active-active with Route53 health checks and latency routing. For RTO=15min: pilot light — replicate data, pre-deploy minimal infra, use Terraform to scale up on failover. For RTO=1hr: warm standby — scaled-down version always running. For RTO=4hr: cold backup — restore from S3 snapshots. Practice: run DR drills quarterly, automate failover with runbooks, test that restored data is actually consistent and usable.

AWS Cost Optimization

Cost Optimization Pillars
Right-sizing → Reserved/Savings Plans → Spot Instances → Storage tiering → Data transfer optimization

Practical Cost Reduction Strategies

# 1. Find oversized EC2 instances (Compute Optimizer)
aws compute-optimizer get-ec2-instance-recommendations \
  --filters name=Finding,values=Overprovisioned \
  --query 'instanceRecommendations[*].{Instance:instanceArn,Current:currentInstanceType,Recommended:recommendationOptions[0].instanceType,Savings:recommendationOptions[0].estimatedMonthlySavings.value}' \
  --output table

# 2. Find unattached EBS volumes
aws ec2 describe-volumes \
  --filters Name=status,Values=available \
  --query 'Volumes[*].{ID:VolumeId,Size:Size,Type:VolumeType,Created:CreateTime}' \
  --output table

# 3. Find idle RDS instances (< 1% CPU for 7 days)
aws cloudwatch get-metric-statistics \
  --namespace AWS/RDS \
  --metric-name CPUUtilization \
  --statistics Average \
  --period 604800 \
  --start-time $(date -d '7 days ago' --iso-8601=seconds) \
  --end-time $(date --iso-8601=seconds) \
  --dimensions Name=DBInstanceIdentifier,Value=my-db

# 4. EBS gp2 → gp3 migration (20% cheaper, better performance)
aws ec2 modify-volume --volume-id vol-xxx --volume-type gp3

# 5. Set S3 lifecycle rules (Terraform)
resource "aws_s3_bucket_lifecycle_configuration" "example" {
  bucket = aws_s3_bucket.logs.id
  rule {
    id     = "log-transition"
    status = "Enabled"
    transition {
      days          = 30
      storage_class = "STANDARD_IA"
    }
    transition {
      days          = 90
      storage_class = "GLACIER"
    }
    expiration {
      days = 365
    }
  }
}

Spot Instance Strategy for Kubernetes

# eks-nodegroup-spot.yaml
apiVersion: eksctl.io/v1alpha5
kind: ClusterConfig
metadata:
  name: prod-cluster
  region: us-east-1
managedNodeGroups:
  # On-demand for critical workloads
  - name: critical-od
    instanceType: m5.xlarge
    minSize: 2
    maxSize: 5
    labels:
      node-type: on-demand
    taints:
      - key: dedicated
        value: critical
        effect: NoSchedule

  # Spot for batch/flexible workloads
  - name: spot-workers
    instanceTypes: [m5.xlarge, m5a.xlarge, m4.xlarge, m5d.xlarge, m5n.xlarge]
    spot: true
    minSize: 0
    maxSize: 20
    labels:
      node-type: spot
    taints:
      - key: spot
        value: "true"
        effect: PreferNoSchedule
# Pod that prefers spot but tolerates on-demand fallback
spec:
  tolerations:
    - key: spot
      operator: Equal
      value: "true"
      effect: PreferNoSchedule
  affinity:
    nodeAffinity:
      preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
        - weight: 80
          preference:
            matchExpressions:
              - key: node-type
                operator: In
                values: [spot]
Q: How do you approach FinOps — bringing cost visibility to engineering teams?
A: Tag all resources with team, service, environment, and cost-center tags enforced via SCP (Service Control Policy) or AWS Config rule. Use AWS Cost Explorer with tag-based cost allocation reports. Feed costs into engineering dashboards (Grafana with CloudWatch billing metrics). Set per-team budget alerts in AWS Budgets with SNS notifications. Review monthly in engineering all-hands — make costs visible, not punitive. Effective approaches: show cost-per-request or cost-per-user metrics, not just absolute dollar amounts; celebrate cost reductions; include cost in PR reviews for infrastructure changes. Right-sizing is usually the biggest win — Compute Optimizer finds overprovisioned resources automatically.

kubectl Advanced Cheat Sheet

# Context & Auth
kubectl config get-contexts
kubectl config use-context prod-context
kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=prod
aws eks update-kubeconfig --name prod-cluster --region us-east-1

# Debugging
kubectl get events --sort-by=.lastTimestamp -n prod
kubectl describe pod  -n prod
kubectl logs  -c  --previous --tail=100
kubectl exec -it  -- /bin/sh
kubectl port-forward svc/myservice 8080:80 -n prod
kubectl top pods -n prod --sort-by=memory
kubectl top nodes

# Resource management
kubectl get pods -o wide --show-labels
kubectl get pods -l app=myapp,env=prod
kubectl get all -n prod
kubectl get pods --field-selector=status.phase=Running
kubectl delete pods --field-selector=status.phase=Failed -n prod

# Rollout management
kubectl rollout status deployment/myapp
kubectl rollout history deployment/myapp
kubectl rollout undo deployment/myapp
kubectl rollout undo deployment/myapp --to-revision=3
kubectl set image deployment/myapp myapp=myapp:v2
kubectl rollout restart deployment/myapp

# Debugging network
kubectl run debug --image=busybox --rm -it --restart=Never -- sh
kubectl run debug --image=nicolaka/netshoot --rm -it --restart=Never -- bash
# Inside pod:
# nslookup kubernetes.default
# curl http://myservice.prod.svc.cluster.local/health
# wget -O- http://10.0.1.5:8080

# Apply with dry run
kubectl apply -f manifest.yaml --dry-run=server
kubectl diff -f manifest.yaml

# Patch
kubectl patch deployment myapp -p '{"spec":{"replicas":5}}'
kubectl patch node node1 -p '{"spec":{"unschedulable":true}}'

# JSONPath queries
kubectl get pods -o jsonpath='{.items[*].metadata.name}'
kubectl get nodes -o jsonpath='{.items[*].status.addresses[?(@.type=="ExternalIP")].address}'

# Drain & cordon (node maintenance)
kubectl cordon node1          # Mark unschedulable
kubectl drain node1 --ignore-daemonsets --delete-emptydir-data --grace-period=300
kubectl uncordon node1

# Force delete stuck namespace
kubectl get namespace stuck-ns -o json | jq '.spec.finalizers = []' | kubectl replace --raw "/api/v1/namespaces/stuck-ns/finalize" -f -
